MANSION --- The Market: The Call of the Castaway --- Scores of private islands are for sale off the North American coast, with prices down 25% from their peak
By Dawn Wotapka
1242 words
2 November 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
M4
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Americans lured by the fantasy of owning a private island don't have to go far asea.

From the sunny Florida Keys up the chilly Canadian coast, values of private islands off the coast of North America have tumbled an estimated 25% or more since the housing downfall, real-estate experts say. Now, prices are starting to attract buyers again.

"There are small signs of a recovery," says Farhad Vladi, president of Vladi Private Islands, a global brokerage based in Hamburg, Germany, that he started 35 years ago. "We have inquiries and that was not so much the case last year."

So far this year, Mr. Vladi has sold 50 islands across North America. That's up from 10 in all of 2011.

There are roughly 21,000 named islands in the U.S., though some are too small for habitation and others too marshy for development, according to the U.S. Geological Survey. Canada also has about 21,000 named islands with scores of unnamed ones, according to Natural Resources Canada, a government agency. Islands that are developed or have the building permits in place that allow developments are the most desirable, as are those with deep-water access.

In September, a buyer paid $950,000 -- a 27% discount off the listing price -- for the two Lower Birch Islands, which span nearly 30 acres in Addison, Maine. The larger island features a three-bedroom, post-and-beam home that uses solar power. In Nova Scotia, the 77-acre Gravel Island, which features a rustic log cabin relying on well water, recently sold for about $400,000, slightly above the asking price.

Islands that don't already have building permits can be a hard sell, as William Scherer is learning. In 1983, his father purchased 47 undeveloped acres with picturesque views located just south of Duck Key in the Florida Keys for $400,000. Because the two islands, known as Tom's Harbor Keys, don't include building permits, he recently listed them for $1.5 million, well below the $10 million he estimates he could command otherwise.

It's unlikely the buyer could get any permits easily. To protect the local habitat, which, includes coral reefs, receiving permission to build in the Keys is complicated, competitive and can take a decade or more. Florida allots the area just 255 annual building permits, and requests are primarily judged on environmental sensitivity, said Christine Hurley, Monroe County's growth-management division director.

Given their location in a federally protected area, the Tom's Harbor islands can't be linked to water, sewer or electrical systems. With no flood insurance or a federally backed mortgage possible, "the owner would be challenged and he'd need to have all cash," Ms. Hurley said.

For islands that can be developed, advances in technology have made it easier and more affordable to generate power, filter water and connect with the outside world. Manufactured housing, long been associated with flimsy trailers, is increasingly popular as the designs look more like a traditional weekend home or vacation cottage.

Still, Ling Graves, president of Hilton Head, S.C.-based Graves Construction Co., estimates that building on a private island costs 40% more than the mainland because manpower and materials have to cross the water. A simple cabin could cost about $100 a square foot, while a high-end custom abode would cost much more.

Because bringing power lines to an island is expensive and requires government permits, Mr. Graves advises using a generator for electricity, requiring about $20,000 for an average home. Expect to spend between $6,000 and $8,000 to install a well for water.

Island construction requires meticulous planning: There won't be any quick trips to the hardware store. "If you forget about it, you've definitely lost a day," he said. "When you're remote, you're remote."

Bob Rainek of Milwaukee has listed three small islands in the Keys. Two of the islands include homes, while the third comes with a docked houseboat. Mr. Rainek, a 63-year-old real-estate investor, says he loves spending time on his islands. "I personally get the feeling of being very unstressed," he says, adding he often catches fish and lobster for dinner. "You have the ultimate waterfront property. Never a day goes by where we don't see dolphins jumping outside our windows."

Mr. Rainek paid $250,000 for his first island 20 years ago, the same price he paid for a second island a decade ago. He hopes they'll sell for around $1 million each. He also aims to nearly double his money on island No. 3, for which he paid $800,000 five years ago. Mr. Rainek says he's looking to retire and give back to the world, possibly by spending time doing humanitarian work in Sudan and Congo.

Potential buyers of private islands generally tend to be wealthy, value privacy, like nature and enjoy boating and other water-related activities. Men are big shoppers, brokers say. For some, owning an island is the ultimate sign of success, one that carries big bragging rights. "It's like the endgame toy," says Chris Krolow, chief executive of Private Islands Inc., a global island brokerage based in Toronto.

Listings website Realtor.com has 460 listings that include an island, with 205 that feature a home. The median price is $950,000, with the cheapest listing, $119,900, in Milan, N.H. At $29.5 million, Little Bokeelia Island south of St. Petersburg, Fla., is the priciest. Mr. Krolow has more than 220 for sale in North America, up from 190 a year ago. He has 60 islands available in Ontario, 28 in Florida and 15 in Maine.

Mr. Vladi urges would-be buyers to start by renting. Then, once they've identified a particular island, he suggests spending time with the mainland locals, who often form a close-knit society. Shoppers should also scope out medical care, which should be within a 90-minute radius of the island.

Check out the plants and animals on the island. "Do you like the flowers? Do you hate snakes? Are there too many mosquitoes?" he says. "You can only find that out if you are there."

While there is no guarantee island values won't fall further, the market is "coming back stronger because prices are more in line with what they should be," Mr. Krolow said.

US
Manufacturing Expands, Construction Spending Rises
By Kathleen Madigan
801 words
1 November 2012
10:57
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The U.S. manufacturing sector expanded last month, but employment weakened, according to data released Thursday by the Institute for Supply Management.

The ISM's manufacturing purchasing managers' index was little changed at 51.7 in October from 51.5 in September. A reading above 50 indicates expansion. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires expected the October PMI to come in at 51.0.

"Comments from the panel this month reflect continued concern over a fragile global economy and soft orders across several manufacturing sectors," the report said.

The October ISM report follows other weak factory surveys for the month. Earlier Thursday, data provider Markit said its own factory index fell to a 37-month low in October.

The ISM indexes were generally mixed.

Demand improved, although the global slowdown hobbled exports. The new-orders index rose to 54.2 from 52.3 in September. The exports index remained in contraction at 48.0 from 48.5.

The production index expanded after two months of contraction, rising to 52.4 from 49.5. The employment index slipped to 52.1 from 54.7.

Manufacturers reported a small easing in cost pressures. The ISM's prices index slowed to 55.0 from 58.0.

Separately, spending on construction projects rose in September as home building jumped, offsetting declines in commercial and government outlays.

Construction spending increased 0.6% to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $851.56 billion, the Commerce Department said. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires expected a 0.5% increase.

The gain, the fifth in the past six months, comes after August building slipped 0.1% to $846.17 billion, according to revised data, compared with an initially reported 0.6% monthly decrease.

Private residential construction increased 2.8% to $285.86 billion, its highest level since December 2008. September spending on private home building was 20.9% higher than a year earlier.

Home building and renovations were an important driver of economic growth in the third quarter. Residential fixed investment has contributed to the gross domestic product for six consecutive quarters after being a drag during the housing bust and financial crisis.

However, the data showed private nonresidential construction—which includes spending on offices, manufacturing and transportation—fell 0.1% to $294.61 billion in September.

Business investment in building contracted in the third quarter for the first time since early 2011, the Commerce Department's GDP report showed.

Total private-sector construction spending increased 1.3% in September, according to Thursday's data.

Public-sector construction fell 0.8% to $271.10 billion during the month to its lowest level since December 2006. Governments spent less on roads, schools and offices.

Federal construction was down 6.2%, and state and local spending fell 0.3%.

Meanwhile, U.S. consumer confidence rose in October to its highest reading since early 2008, according to a report released Thursday.

The Conference Board, a private research group, said its index of consumer confidence increased to 72.2 last month from a revised 68.4 in September, which originally had been reported as a more optimistic 70.3.

The latest index is just below the 73.0 expected by economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires but is the highest confidence reading since February 2008.

"Consumers were considerably more positive in their assessment of current conditions, with the improvements in the job market as the major driver," said Lynn Franco, director of economic indicators at the board.

The board usually publishes its confidence report on the last Tuesday of the month, but the October report was delayed until Thursday because of superstorm Sandy.

Within the Conference Board's report, the present-situation index, a gauge of consumers' assessment of current economic conditions, advanced to 56.2 from a revised 48.7, first reported as 50.2.

Consumer expectations for economic activity over the next six months edged up to 82.9 from a revised 81.5, originally put at 83.7.

Consumers were more upbeat about current labor-market conditions, which is a positive indicator for the October payrolls report scheduled for Friday.

The board's survey showed 10.3% of respondents now think jobs are "plentiful." That is up from 8.1% in September and the highest reading in at least a year. Another 39.4% think jobs are "hard to get," down from 40.7% last month.

Consumers' expectations about future job markets are mixed. The results show that 19.2% think there will be more jobs in the next six months, up from 18.1% in September. But 20.3% think there will be fewer jobs, up from 18.7% in September.

Eric Morath and Sarah Portlock contributed to this article.

US
Manufacturers Notch Modest Gains
By Josh Mitchell
696 words
1 November 2012
16:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The U.S. factory sector expanded modestly last month but producers remain cautious about hiring and face a sustained slump in exports.

The Institute for Supply Management said Thursday that its closely watched index ticked up to 51.7 in October from 51.5 a month earlier. The index is based on a survey of purchasing managers who order everything for factories from raw materials to light bulbs. Readings over 50 indicate growth.

The upturn shows manufacturers shifting from a summer lull, when the index contracted for three consecutive months.

Meanwhile, manufacturing data from around the world continued to paint a picture of contraction and slow growth. An official purchasing managers' index from China just barely moved back into expansionary territory last month, but a private measure produced by HSBC and Markit still indicated contraction. Meanwhile, the factory sectors in Japan and South Korea continued to shrink. Much of Europe won't report manufacturing data until Friday, but indexes in the U.K., Austria and the Netherlands showed shrinking manufacturing sectors.

U.S. factories are being helped by spending by American consumers, who appear to be gaining confidence in the economy as more people get jobs, incomes gradually rise and rising home sales slowly lift property values. The Conference Board said Thursday that its index of consumer confidence rose to a four-and-a-half-year high of 72.2 in October, up from 68.4 in September.

Those reports, along with others Thursday, hint at some momentum in the U.S. recovery. Payroll firm Automatic Data Processing Inc. said its newly revamped survey showed the economy added 158,000 private-sector jobs in October, a solid if not sterling clip.

Meantime, the number of American workers filing first-time claims for unemployment benefits fell by 9,000 last week to a seasonally adjusted 363,000, the Labor Department said, indicating that layoffs are slowing. The four-week moving average, which smooths out weekly volatility, also fell, to 367,250.

The manufacturing report provided a small dose of relief as it showed that overall demand in the U.S. remains stable compared with economies around the globe, particularly in recession-ravaged Europe. But the report also contained red flags, showing that businesses are hunkering down and that global economic troubles continue to hurt U.S. exports.

Perhaps most promising was a measure of new orders, which rose nearly two percentage points to 54.2. Such an increase usually means that demand is rising and that factories will need to boost production in coming months. That already has happened, with the gauge for production rising 2.9 percentage points to 52.4.

But the industry growth came from only eight of 18 sectors—mainly those tied to consumer goods, such as furniture, apparel and food. Factories that generally serve business customers—makers of machinery, electrical equipment and transportation equipment—reported declines in business. That suggests that companies are holding off on purchases of big-ticket items amid growing uncertainty about the global economy and in the U.S., said Brad Holcomb, who oversees the ISM manufacturing survey. The report showed that a measure of exports slipped half a point to 48.0, while another gauge, which measures hiring, slowed.

Mr. Holcomb said manufacturers might see a boost in demand in coming months as communities start repairing storm damage from Sandy, but that the overall outlook is uncertain.

Economist Paul Dales of Capital Economics said the outlook for manufacturers remains "bleak." "Our view that the global economy will remain weak next year implies that manufacturers will continue to struggle," Mr. Dales wrote in a note to clients. "But there is no reason why the housing recovery can't continue to strengthen at the same time."

The pickup in the housing market is leading to higher construction spending as companies build homes to meet stronger demand. Construction spending rose to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $851.6 billion in September, a rise of 0.6% from a month earlier and 7.8% from a year ago, the Commerce Department said.

U.S. News: Home Prices Climb Across Country
By Conor Dougherty
435 words
31 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The U.S. housing market continued to gain steam in August, with new data showing that home-price increases accelerated over the month while also spreading to more cities across the country.

The Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller 20-city home-price index rose 2.0% in August from a year earlier, an acceleration from the 1.2% annual increase in July and the fastest pace in two years, according to a report released Tuesday. Prices were up in 17 of the 20 metropolitan areas tracked by the index, compared with 16 in the report for July and three for January.

The housing recovery -- which is being driven by a combination of rising demand, declining inventory and low interest rates -- has been a bright spot in a sluggish economy still trying to find its footing.

Tuesday's report was encouraging to economists because continued and accelerating price growth, at a time when prices gains are usually slowing down after the peak summer sales season, suggests the housing recovery has become self-reinforcing.

"This is a real recovery with legs," said Ellen Zentner, an economist at Nomura Securities. "What's especially encouraging is that price gains are deepening across the country."

Though closely followed by economists and the markets, the Case-Shiller index is a lagging indicator of values -- a moving average now two months old. Still, other home-value and home-price indicators suggest price gains have continued into September and October -- a possible explanation for why consumer sentiment, though low by historical standards, continues to improve.

The U.S. housing market has recovered largely thanks to the declining number of foreclosures, bank sales and other "distressed" homes that sell at big discounts and drag down prices. Meanwhile, as the cost of homeownership has fallen to the point where monthly rent exceeds mortgage payments in some markets, there has been a surge in demand at the lower end of the market.

Still, with inventory dwindling and household incomes still under pressure, economists caution that price gains will soon flatten out if job and income growth don't pick up.

Price gains generally have been stronger in markets that were hit hard by the housing bust. Leading the pack was Phoenix, where prices were up 18.8% from a year earlier. Detroit, Minneapolis and Miami all posted year-to-year gains above 6%. The three cities with year-to-year declines were Atlanta, New York and Chicago.

Housing Bulls Shrug Off Skeptics, Hold Ground
By Alexandra Scaggs
945 words
30 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Some investors are sticking to their bullish bets on home builders, despite a number of warnings from Wall Street analysts that the massive rally in the stocks is overdone.

The optimists say they believe the recovery of the U.S. housing market still is in its initial phase, leaving plenty of room for the stocks to keep rising.

The gains so far have been startling. PulteGroup Inc., the largest U.S. home builder by revenue, is up 214% in the past 12 months, when new-home sales began rising consistently. It also is the best performer in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index over the past year. Lennar Corp., the third-largest U.S. home builder by revenue, is up 117% in 12 months. In the same period, the S&P 500 is up 10%.

"Home sales tend to move higher over a five-year cycle, and this is year one," says Kirk Mentzer, director of investment research with Huntington Asset Advisors, a $14 billion fund in Columbus, Ohio. "There's still a ways to go, even if the rise from here is just half of the current rally." The firm bought D.R. Horton Inc., a home builder in Fort Worth, Texas, at the end of 2011, and is hanging on even after a 71% run-up this year.

Data in recent months have indicated the pace of recovery has accelerated. New-home sales jumped 5.7% in September to their highest level in over two years. The Commerce Department said recently that housing starts had risen to 872,000, the highest seasonally adjusted annual rate in four years.

Investors are more optimistic about the housing sector than they are about the broader economy, in part because of the recent action by the Federal Reserve to further bolster the market by purchasing mortgage-backed securities. Both analysts and investors expect the housing market to continue to improve, though they note that if the recovery comes to a halt, shares of companies sensitive to the housing market -- including those of builders -- are likely to sell off.

Investors have poured $653.7 million into the iShares Dow Jones U.S. Home Construction Index Fund so far in 2012, putting it in the top 5% of exchange-traded funds in terms of inflows, according to Morningstar.

And home builders have shown resilience during the recent swoon in the broader market. The Dow Jones U.S. Home Construction Index has lost 0.5% over the past two weeks, compared with a 3.3% drop for the S&P 500

Mr. Mentzer and other bullish investors point to history to bolster their position. During the housing upturn from 2001 through the end of 2005, shares of custom builder KB Home surged 357%. Since September 2011, they are up a relatively modest 145%. And because the housing-market slump of recent years was the most severe on record, there is more room for home-builder shares to rise this time around, investors say.

Sorin Roibu, a housing analyst for money manager Turner Investment Partners, which oversees nearly $12 billion, believes with the labor market in the U.S. still slack, home builders will have an easier time managing costs in the current upswing. He expects bigger margins as a result.

He also says investors shouldn't underestimate the power of market sentiment, especially at a time when technology and other sectors are facing headwinds. "If you look at home-building stocks, they're the best direct play on the U.S. housing recovery, and everybody wants to play it," Mr. Roibu says.

The magnitude of the stock gains has elicited heavy skepticism on Wall Street, where analysts typically are seen as cheerleaders for stocks.

Megan McGrath, executive director and home-building analyst at brokerage MKM Partners, this month downgraded Ryland Group, a major builder on the West Coast, to sell, and cut her ratings on D.R. Horton and Lennar to neutral.

"You can still be bullish on housing but not necessarily the home-builder stocks," Ms. McGrath says. "We're still in the early stages of recovery, but these stocks are up around 150% in a year, so we thought, look, if you've made the trade and you've made some money, maybe it's time to take some profits."

Susquehanna Financial Group analyst Jack Micenko believes shares have become too expensive. Mr. Micenko says that for shares to warrant their current valuations, the U.S. will need to build an average of 2.6 million new single-family homes a year from 2013 through 2016. During the peak of new-home construction in 2006, the U.S. built 1.6 million new single-family homes. He has a negative outlook on the group.

But today's annual rate of 872,000 homes is still well short of the norm over the past half century. On average, builders have started construction on about 1.5 million new homes a year since 1959.

Mark Luschini, chief investment strategist with Janney Montgomery Scott LLC, argues that the current level of new-home sales is so low that there still is nowhere for home builders to go but up. "At the end of the day, homes are being sold at an appallingly low rate. . . . That still bodes well for home builders," he said. The firm, which oversees $55 billion in its Parker/Hunter Asset Management arm, is keeping its position in the Dow Jones U.S. Home Construction Index steady.

Business
Home Prices Climb Across Country
By Conor Dougherty
426 words
30 October 2012
18:28
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The U.S. housing market continued to gain steam in August, with new data showing that home-price increases accelerated over the month while also spreading to more cities across the country.

The Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller 20-city home-price index rose 2.0% in August from a year earlier, an acceleration from the 1.2% annual increase in July and the fastest pace in two years, according to a report released Tuesday. Prices were up in 17 of the 20 metropolitan areas tracked by the index, compared with 16 in the report for July and three for January.

The housing recovery—which is being driven by a combination of rising demand, declining inventory and low interest rates—has been a bright spot in a sluggish economy still trying to find its footing.

Tuesday's report was encouraging to economists because continued and accelerating price growth, at a time when prices gains are usually slowing down after the peak summer sales season, suggests the housing recovery has become self-reinforcing.

"This is a real recovery with legs," said Ellen Zentner, an economist at Nomura Securities. "What's especially encouraging is that price gains are deepening across the country."

Though closely followed by economists and the markets, the Case-Shiller index is a lagging indicator of values—a moving average now two months old. Still, other home-value and home-price indicators suggest price gains have continued into September and October—a possible explanation for why consumer sentiment, though low by historical standards, continues to improve.

The U.S. housing market has recovered largely thanks to the declining number of foreclosures, bank sales and other "distressed" homes that sell at big discounts and drag down prices. Meanwhile, as the cost of homeownership has fallen to the point where monthly rent exceeds mortgage payments in some markets, there has been a surge in demand at the lower end of the market.

Still, with inventory dwindling and household incomes still under pressure, economists caution that price gains will soon flatten out if job and income growth don't pick up.

Price gains generally have been stronger in markets that were hit hard by the housing bust. Leading the pack was Phoenix, where prices were up 18.8% from a year earlier. Detroit, Minneapolis and Miami all posted year-to-year gains above 6%. The three cities with year-to-year declines were Atlanta, New York and Chicago.

The Intelligent Investor: In a Low-Rate World, Everything Is a Real-Estate Play
By Jason Zweig
790 words
27 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Real estate is red-hot.

Funds specializing in real-estate investment trusts, or REITs, have taken in $10.9 billion in new money this year through Sept. 30, according to Morningstar, the financial-research firm. That is more than REIT funds took in for all of 2010 and 2011 combined.

Desperate for income, investors can't get enough high-yielding REITs -- and Wall Street is rushing to supply them. Companies are lining up to convert into REITs, a step that requires approval from the Internal Revenue Service.

So the market is changing -- and investors should temper their expectations accordingly.

REITs must prove to the IRS that their main business is investing in real estate. They also must pay out no less than 90% of their taxable income as dividends; as a result, REITs generally don't owe federal income tax.

With REITs yield almost 4%, it's no wonder yield-parched investors are lapping them up.

And no wonder companies that aren't REITs want to be. In the pipeline for conversion: Cincinnati Bell's subsidiary CyrusOne, Corrections Corp. of America, Crown Castle International, Equinix, Geo Group, Iron Mountain, Lamar Advertising, and SBA Communications. These companies operate cloud-computing data centers, cellphone towers, prisons, billboards and document-storage facilities.

Mike Kirby, director of research at Green Street Advisors, a research firm in Newport Beach, Calif., says the pending conversions are "a bit of a stretch" from the most familiar forms of real estate held by REITs: office, industrial, residential and retail properties.

The companies cite the potential for tax savings, increased dividends and higher stock valuations. Iron Mountain, for example, estimates it could save up to $130 million on taxes annually and roughly double its dividend. The move "is the best approach to execute our real-estate business and related capital-allocation strategies," the company said.

On average, says Ross Smotrich, a REIT analyst at Barclays, these stocks have popped roughly 40% since the companies started discussing conversion to REIT status.

Says Jeff Middleswart, president of Behind the Numbers, a Dallas-based research firm: "REITs are hot, so people are trying to convert things that aren't hot into REITs."

American Tower, a cell-tower firm, converted last Dec. 31 and is up more than 20% since, versus 14% for the average REIT stock. On the other hand, Weyerhaeuser, the timber company, switched in 2010 and has gained nearly 50% this year -- but remains below its price five years ago.

Scott Westphal, an analyst at Cornerstone Real Estate Advisers in Stamford, Conn., says 10 to 20 companies could convert to REITs in the next several years. "There will be some great opportunities coming in," he says. "Unfortunately, some of these sectors have a checkered past."

Prisons, cell towers and golf courses were turned into REITs in the late 1990s, Mr. Westphal says -- with "pretty dreadful" results that in some cases produced losses of 90% or more.

Corrections Corp. did a REIT conversion once before, in 1999, at more than $70 per share. By 2000, the stock was below $1 and the conversion was reversed.

The company said this year that its pending REIT conversion could provide "a more tax-efficient structure with higher cash flow."

"The key question," says Mr. Smotrich of Barclays, "is whether these conversions will have the strength in the underlying business that will support the [stock prices] they're hoping for."

For investors, REITs can reduce the risks of a stock-and-bond portfolio and protect against the ravages of inflation. Because they own properties whose values don't move in sync with housing, REITs are a hedge against declining home values.

The best way to own REITs is through a low-cost index or exchange-traded fund that spans all real-estate sectors and geographic areas, like the Vanguard REIT ETF or the SPDR Dow Jones REIT ETF. The indexes the funds track are unlikely to add REIT conversions soon, analysts say.

While owning REITs is a good idea, panic buying isn't. Many investors are dumping money-market funds or bond funds and replacing them with higher-yielding REITs, says Morningstar analyst Michael Rawson. But REITs aren't bonds; the FTSE NAREIT Equity REITs Index, a benchmark of more than 120 of these stocks, lost 37.7% in 2008, when U.S. Treasury bonds had a positive 13.7% return.

Why plunge in with both feet when you can tiptoe in? It's wiser to buy over time rather than all at once.

---

U.S. News: High Costs Bedevil Builders --- Shortages of Lumber, Land and Labor Push Up Prices of New-Home Construction
By Robbie Whelan
950 words
26 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

New-home construction has picked up momentum in recent months after years of contraction. But the industry is facing a fresh challenge: building costs are going through the roof.

Builders are seeing the prices of materials, labor and land rise due to shortages and reduced capacity among manufacturers, subcontractors and developers. Some industry veterans say the price increases could be temporary, noting that supply-demand imbalances often occur early in a recovery. That happens in part because the industries that fuel the engines of the construction trade are wary about ramping up until they are sure the recovery is sustainable.

But rising costs mean many builders will try to pass along price increases to consumers. If too many potential new-home buyers opt instead for an older home, that could have implications for the economy. Sales of new homes provide a much bigger lift to economic growth than sales of older ones. The National Association of Home Builders estimates that each new home built generates about three new jobs.

"If somebody buys a new home, you've got to hire a bunch of construction workers to go build it," said Michael Hanson, senior U.S. economist with Bank of America, senior U.S. economist with Merrill Lynch. If fewer new homes sell, he said, "that would mean less of a boost" to gross domestic product.

The Census Bureau reported Wednesday that the median price of a new home was $242,400 in September, up 11.5% from a year ago. The median price of an existing home was $183,900 in September, up 11.3% from a year earlier.

The cost of framing lumber, which makes up nearly one-sixth of the total construction cost of a home, has risen 21% in the past year. Makers of drywall, which is used to build interior walls, have boosted prices 25% since January and signaled more increases to come early next year. In addition, builders are paying more for land and labor.

"Builders have their backs against the wall," said Robert Denk, senior economist at the NAHB. In a market that has just begun to recover, "it's a very difficult time for builders to try and pass on costs to consumers."

Yet some builders are doing just that. Carmeter and Curtis Lard started shopping for a new house in June, hoping to find a three-bedroom in Otay Ranch, a suburb of San Diego with good schools.

The first home that caught their interest was built by McMillin Homes and was selling for $401,000 -- the top end of their price range. Three weeks later, after the couple had qualified for a mortgage, the same model was selling for $418,000. The couple visited several other new-home communities but couldn't find anything in their price range and gave up in August. Now, they are shopping for an older home.

"By the time we were qualified, the house was out of reach," Ms. Lard said. "We didn't realize it would be that difficult to buy here."

Timber companies and drywall manufacturers, for example, laid off thousands of workers and idled capacity at plants when the downturn hit and want to see at least six months of rising demand before reversing course. (Lumber shortages are also the result of an infestation of bark beetles that has destroyed millions of acres of pine trees in the U.S. and Canada.)

Through the first half of 2012, the lumber available to U.S. builders measured 18.2 billion board feet, down about 45% from the peak of production in 2005. Shawn Church, editor of Random Lengths, a trade publication that tracks the price of wood, said "production is coming back from the 2009 trough" of 16 billion board feet produced through the first half of that year, "but very slowly."

Drywall manufacturers, in addition to raising prices, have stopped providing "job quotes," which allow builders and contractors to lock in at low prices with their suppliers, for projects that might last several years.

"Any time there's a price increase at the manufacturer level and we go to pass it along, we get a lot of questions from buyers," said Ira Horner, a manager at Kent Gypsum Supply Inc., a drywall distributor in Kent, Wash. "As the price of materials goes up, it definitely is going to hurt the number of projects that take place."

Land also is in short supply, at least in the places where consumers want to live, near jobs and good schools. In Gilbert, Ariz., near Phoenix, builders including Lennar Corp., Shea Homes and Meritage Homes Corp. recently purchased 203 acres of raw land in a development called The Bridges, paying between $126,000 and $150,000 an acre. In the Spring of 2011, some investors were paying as little as $77,763 an acre for the same land.

Labor is another problem. Between 2007 and 2011, more than 2.1 million construction workers lost their jobs and many switched to jobs in other industries. "These are folks who lost their jobs, waited around for years and then found something else," said Megan McGrath, a home builder analyst with MKM Partners. "The trick is getting the skilled labor force to come back."

That will be easy in some regions, but not everywhere. Jonathan Jaffe, Lennar's chief operating officer, said in a recent conference call that labor shortages are most acute in Phoenix, Texas and parts of Florida.

Currency Markets
Hong Kong Holds Lid on Its Dollar
By Fiona Law
573 words
25 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
22
English
Copyright © 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Hong Kong's banking system is awash in cash, pushing lending rates down and threatening to push up property prices, as the de facto central bank continues to intervene in the currency market.

The Hong Kong Monetary Authority has sold a combined 14.36 billion Hong Kong dollars (US$1.85 billion) in four interventions since Friday as it seeks to keep the currency from strengthening out of its trading band against the U.S. dollar. The HKMA said Wednesday it expects funds will continue to flow into the Hong Kong dollar on a net basis "for some time" and pledged to maintain the currency's stability.

Cash has surged into the banking system from the intervention, and as overseas fund managers, more willing to take on risk as a result of global stimulus measures, snap up the city's stocks. As a result, Hong Kong's benchmark three-month interbank lending rate has fallen to 0.25%, from 0.35% before the first intervention, and 0.5% two weeks ago. When that rate falls, it brings down benchmark borrowing rates for companies, and mortgage rates for individuals.

Barclays said that if interest rates remain low, there likely will be more lending, which would support the property market. The city's housing prices have already risen 16% from the start of the year through to August. When borrowing rates fell from 4% to below 1% in 2009, home prices jumped 29%.

Still, "the rates drop hasn't been drastic, and its impact to the financial market wouldn't be as substantial as it was during 2008-2009," said Tommy Ong, head of wealth-management solutions at DBS Group Holdings Ltd.'s Hong Kong unit.

A measure of liquidity among banks in Hong Kong, the so-called aggregate balance, has climbed to HK$163 billion, from around HK$149 billion before the intervention Friday. The last time the HKMA intervened was in 2008 and 2009, when the Hong Kong dollar was seen as a haven during market turmoil.

Eddie Yue, deputy chief executive of the HKMA, said Wednesday that the U.S. stimulus measures announced in September may trigger demand for Asian currencies, equities and bonds. Some investors who have held U.S. dollars are switching back to the local unit as investors world-wide become more willing to take on risk, he added.

This time, the HKMA has been mostly stepping in during the U.S. day, when trading volume is less, making it easier for speculators to move the market. The Hong Kong dollar is allowed to trade between HK$7.7500 and HK$7.8500 per U.S. dollar. Late Wednesday in Hong Kong, the rate was HK$7.7505.

One-year forward contracts for Hong Kong dollars were priced at HK$7.7440 per dollar late Wednesday, a level beyond the strong end of the trading band. Still, few are predicting any quick change to the peg, which was created in 1983 as confidence in the former British colony wavered ahead of its handover to China in 1997.

"The cost of change is substantial, while the benefits are highly uncertain," said Robert Minikin, senior foreign-exchange strategist at Standard Chartered PLC's Hong Kong unit. "Any change to the peg will be long-delayed until the yuan becomes fully convertible."

The Wall Street Journal Online

Bargain buyers were out in force in the Hamptons over the summer, turning the period into the strongest summer season for sales since 2006.

But median prices fell by 10% during the third quarter compared with the year-earlier quarter, as sales of the most-expensive homes—mansions that often come with ocean-front bragging rights—lagged behind, according to new market reports.

The highest price sale during the quarter was an $18.6 million sale of a house in Wainscott near the ocean. That was far below the peak price for the year—a $28.5 million ocean front house in Southampton Village sold by Marc Rowan, a co-founder of private-equity firm Apollo Global Management. That sale closed in January.

Susan Breitenbach, a top-selling East End broker, said that since the third quarter ended Sept. 30, there have been "a lot of high-end people" looking at houses. She said that a spate of big deals, including some topping $20 million, would close before the end of the year.

"You are going to see a lot of properties trading during the last quarter," she said.

The market reports show that a sales rebound in the Hamptons that began earlier this year, especially among properties priced below $1 million on the South Fork, is still going strong. Those properties are often a cottage or small home, far from the beach.

Analysts said the rebound in deals sub-$1 million deals was driven by falling interest rates and declining prices as enticing properties pushed more below the $1 million level.

In the peak spring season, total sales of 539 were the highest for any quarter since 2007. In the latest quarter there were 405 sales recorded, the highest for the third quarter since 2006, according a market report prepared by Jonathan Miller, an appraiser and president of Miller Samuel Inc., for Prudential Douglas Elliman.

The median price of a Hampton's home was $765,000 In the third quarter, down 10% from both the second quarter and the third quarter of 2011, Mr. Miller's report said. That was still 30% below the peak prices during the boom years.

The average price was $1.43 million, down 18% from the year-earlier third quarter.

Gregory J. Heym, an economist who prepared Hamptons market reports for Brown Harris Stevens and Halstead Property, said that closings were likely to pick up toward the end of the year, as sellers rushed to complete deals before increased capital-gains tax rates scheduled to go into effect next year.

He said that 60% of sales were below $1 million in the latest quarter compared with 57% during the same quarter last year. At the same time, there were 19 sales of $4 million or more compared with 23 in the year-earlier period.

Mr. Miller said that as elsewhere inventory in the Hamptons has been falling, leading to the possibility that prices may rise in the months ahead, even without an improvement in the outlook for the economy.

New-Home Sales Rise as Housing Market Strengthens
By Robbie Whelan
512 words
25 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A5
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Sales of new homes jumped in September to their highest level in more than two years, the latest sign that the housing market is on firmer ground after a bruising five-year downturn.

Builders were on pace to sell new single-family homes at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 389,000 units in September, the Commerce Department said Wednesday, up 5.7% from August, and 27.1% higher than September 2011's annualized pace of 306,000. The level is the highest since April 2010, when first-time home buyers were rushing to qualify for a tax credit.

The median price of a new home was $242,400 in September, up 11.5% from a year earlier, but down from $250,400 in August.

Economists pointed to the gains as a sign that the fundamentals of the economy are improving, as consumers become confident and the unemployment rate improves.

"September's rise in new home sales is another sign that home buyers are becoming more willing and more able to splash out on a new home," wrote Paul Diggle, an economist with Capital Economics in London, in a research note.

Despite the gains, the home-building industry is still stuck in low gear compared with the average sales pace of 845,000 new homes a year reached between 1990 and 2006. New-home sales peaked in July 2005, when they hit an annual pace of nearly 1.4 million, and declined sharply before the financial crisis. Last year's level of 306,000 sales was the lowest on record.

"Levels of activity in the new housing market remain quite weak by historic standards," wrote J.P. Morgan economist Daniel Silver.

Another concern is the inventory of new homes for sale, which fell to an all-time low of 143,000 houses in August, and rose only slightly in September, to 145,000. At the current pace of sales, that represents a 4.5-month supply of homes, the lowest level of inventory since October 2005.

In response, builders have stepped up construction in recent months. The Commerce Department last week said housing starts had risen to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 872,000 new homes, the highest level in four years. Some economists warn that the building industry may be ramping up too quickly and getting ahead of consumer demand for new homes.

"The improvement in demand for new homes may not rise sufficiently to justify the accelerating in construction activity in the past few months," Millan Mulraine, a strategist with TD Securities, wrote Wednesday. "As a result, new home prices are likely to come under further pressure as some of the premium over existing homes (which continue to attract demand) is removed."

September's new-home sales rose in three of four U.S. regions. Sales grew 16.7% in the Northeast, 16.8% in the South and 3.9% in the West but dropped 37.3% in the Midwest.

The Property Report
Deal of the Week: Tech Boosts San Francisco
By Eliot Brown
776 words
24 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A booming tech sector in San Francisco has made its office-building owners the envy of commercial real-estate industry.

Now developers are starting to move in.

Houston-based Hines and office giant Boston Properties announced they would partner to pay $185 million for a development site where the two companies plan a 1,070-foot office tower that would be the tallest building on the West Coast.

The Transbay Tower just south of the city's financial district would be finished at the earliest by 2016. The venture hasn't decided whether it would build on speculation, meaning it would move forward without tenant commitments, said Paul Paradis, senior managing director at Hines.

"It's a great market that's very constrained for space," Mr. Paradis said. "We're very optimistic right now."

In addition, the growing West Coast real-estate investment trust Kilroy Realty Corp. said it has purchased a development site a block away at 350 Mission St., where it plans to develop a 27-story, 400,000-square-foot office tower.

With or without a tenant, the company plans to move ahead in coming months on the Skidmore Owings & Merrill-designed glass building, said John Kilroy Jr., chief executive. "We can beat everybody to market," Mr. Kilroy said.

The deals come as empty San Francisco offices have been filling up over the past two-plus years thanks to a social media and app-fueled recovery. Companies that didn't exist six years ago -- such as Dropbox Inc. and room-rental service Airbnb Inc. -- have recently leased tens of thousands of feet and are rapidly growing.

Rents have shot up 50% since their nadir in the first quarter of 2010, to an average of $50.36 a square foot, according to real-estate services firm Jones Lang LaSalle Inc. Vacancy is down to 12.7% from 17.8% in mid-2010.

"Competition for the space has been so fierce -- they're overbidding for offices and taking whatever they can," Julia Georgules, a research analyst at Jones Lang LaSalle, said of tech firms.

But so far, office development has been restrained in the city given that banks have been reluctant to lend for new buildings. Only one large speculative project is under way by Tishman Speyer Properties LP and the asset management division of J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.

Instead, many developers have been focused on converting old warehouses into the kind of hip offices sought by tech firms.

But with vacancy falling steadily and technology companies expanding, developers believe there will be demand for new construction. "When the tide rises, all boats rise," Mort Zuckerman, Boston Properties' chairman, said.

The site for the Transbay Tower is near an under-construction transportation hub known as the Transbay Transit Center. Hines has been interested in the site for years and initially struck a deal before the downturn to buy in from the public Transbay Joint Powers Authority for $350 million. After the recession, it renegotiated that deal to the current $185 million price. Earlier this year, it tapped real-estate services firm Eastdil Secured LLC to search for an equity partner to buy the land.

What isn't clear is whether the 1.4 million-square-foot development would attract technology companies, which seem to prefer older buildings with large, open floors. With banks in contraction mode, demand for the traditional skyscrapers in downtown San Francisco has lagged behind the more tech-heavy areas such as the South of Market neighborhood lately.

Mr. Paradis, of Hines, said while some may prefer that type of space, he has seen significant interest in the project from technology firms, as well as financial firms that have lease expirations in coming years.

Meanwhile, Kilroy's move to develop 350 Mission represents a shift for the company, which in 2010 and 2011 was one of the most aggressive acquirers of office buildings in San Francisco.

The company is now focusing more on development, in large part because values for well-occupied buildings, known as "core" properties, have returned to near-peak levels.

"We can't find core that is priced to our liking," Mr. Kilroy said. "The yields are so low."

The higher prices, in turn, have led more owners to sell. San Francisco saw more than $3.9 billion in sales of office buildings through the third quarter of 2012, up 58% from the same period in 2011, according to research firm Real Capital Analytics Inc.

The Property Report
J.P. Morgan Gets Stung by China Downturn --- Fund Run by Bank Loses High-End Residential Tower to Foreclosure as Rules on Property Purchases Hit Luxury Market
By Esther Fung
669 words
24 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

SHANGHAI -- A luxury apartment building that is one of the most visible casualties of China's high-end property bust is up for auction in the northeast city of Dalian, after a bank foreclosed on the property.

The 48-floor Park Central was owned by a fund run by J.P. Morgan Asset Management, which invested $80 million in the property. But the asset manager lost the property when U.K.-based lender Standard Chartered PLC, which provided the majority of a roughly $140 million loan, foreclosed over the summer after sluggish sales put it in violation of loan covenants, according to people with knowledge of the matter.

The loss, which came after China tightened rules on buying luxury apartments, shows how exposed some European and U.S. investors are to Chinese real estate. Several potential investors have been offered the property, but no deal has been reached, the people said.

Standard Chartered and J.P. Morgan Asset Management, a unit of J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., declined to comment.

China recently has shown signs of revival in its property market, yet problems remain evident. Property developers such as Greentown China Holdings Ltd. and SPG Land Holdings Ltd. that had focused on high-end homes, have been offloading some of their assets to other developers as cash flow tightened due to government moves to cool the property market. In June, debt-laden Greentown China raised $657 million by selling stock and issuing convertible bonds to shareholder Wharf (Holdings) Ltd.

The government moved in 2010 to slow construction of luxury housing -- through credit curbs, limits on buying multiple properties and higher down-payment requirements -- and sought to push developers toward building for first-time buyers and those upgrading to bigger homes.

Like upwardly mobile Chinese, foreign investors have preferred luxury properties in China due to the higher margins they can fetch compared with the mass-market, cookie-cutter apartments.

But skeptics warn that it will take years for the market to absorb an oversupply of fancy apartments and villas, many left empty by their investor buyers, and "ghost cities" of unoccupied blocks.

"Around three to four years ago you could already see that the government was clamping down on the luxury sector, but some thought that the government was going to leave the top-tier residential sector untouched since it had nothing to do with the mass market. That was misguided," said one person with knowledge of the matter.

The overall level of foreclosure activity in China is unclear. There is little information in the country regarding the volume of distressed real-estate assets.

But high-end residential projects such as Park Central, one of the top buildings in seaside Dalian, have been among the hardest hit since China launched its property tightening campaign.

J.P. Morgan Asset Management took over Park Central during its construction as the market showed signs of froth. The company started a $600 million Greater China property fund in 2008 and invested in projects across the country, including in Beijing, Shanghai and Nanjing. Its projects elsewhere in China don't appear to be in distress, the people said.

The units in Park Central haven't sold well since they went on the market in September 2011. In addition to the government tightening measures, the sales weakness was partly because the units were too big and costly, according to the people with knowledge of the matter and real-estate agents in the city.

Standard Chartered foreclosed on the property after J.P. Morgan breached some loan covenants due to poor sales, said one person. CBRE Group, the broker hired to market Park Central, is working with Standard Chartered to seek potential buyers, the people said.

The Property Report
College Housing Firms Aim Upscale
By Dawn Wotapka
878 words
24 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The communities surrounding college campuses have long been filled with houses catering to students who have tired of living in dorms.

Now, public companies that operate student housing are trying to break into that market. In the past, companies like American Campus Communities Inc. and Education Realty Trust Inc. have mostly developed apartment buildings near colleges that offered students private rooms and bathrooms and a long list of amenities, including resort-style pools and tanning beds.

These days the companies have begun to build upscale houses with bedrooms clustered around gourmet kitchens and access to amenity-filled clubhouses. Known as cottage-style housing, the relatively new product is becoming popular with operators and students.

Nationwide, there are 35 cottage communities with nearly 19,000 beds. An additional 18 are under way or in the works, with roughly 12,000 beds, said Wes Rogers, chief executive of Landmark Properties Inc., which has built roughly one-third of the cottages in the U.S. While cottage-style housing represents a small percentage of the nearly 500,000 beds controlled by the sector's top companies, industry watchers expect the bed count to increase as the product catches on.

"It's sort of the next evolution of student housing," Mr. Rogers said. "You don't have neighbors above you like in a traditional dorm environment. You literally live in your own house."

Developers are building these properties to house an expanding student population: More than three million high-school students are expected to graduate annually until the 2018-19 academic year, well above the roughly 2.5 million graduating in 1993-1994, according to the Department of Education.

Moreover, universities don't have enough beds and much of the current supply, tall towers with communal bathrooms, has lost favor among the McMansion generation. Schools, many struggling with budget cuts, can't afford to build new dorms.

Operators like cottages because the rooms typically command rents at least 10% higher than other off-campus housing. And students tend to like them: Landmark had waiting lists in half of its 16 communities this academic year. Its cottage product is 96% leased, compared with 93% for apartment-style housing.

But some industry watchers fear students' increased attention to debt could be pushing them to cut costs by living at home. Education Realty Trust, also known as EdR, surprised the industry this month when it reported an occupancy rate of 90.5% for this academic year, down from 94.7% a year ago.

Rents increased 5.1%, possibly pushing students to seek cheaper digs. Should the economy weaken, students could continue trading down. Every developer is at risk of beds flooding the market, forcing them to cut rates. Also, competitors are building closer to campus, where students usually want to live.

Partly due to these concerns, the sector's stock-price growth has slowed. Last year, the stocks of both American Campus and Education Realty gained more than 35%, more than double the apartment sector's returns. This year, Education Realty has gained 1.7%, while American Campus has advanced 4.1%. Stocks in the apartment sector are up about 4%.

Jeffrey Kolitch, portfolio manager of the Baron Real Estate Fund, which invests in both American Campus and Education Realty, said he isn't worried about the stocks. "I wouldn't send the alarm signals on these companies," he said. "The stocks were rock stars last year. They're just taking a pause."

Others aren't so sure. J.P. Morgan analyst Anthony Paolone said he likes the sector's long-term potential, but there could be "more juice" with other companies, such as warehouse firm Prologis Inc. and mall company Simon Property Group Inc.

Still, cottage-housing deal activity is intensifying. In September, American Campus, the nation's largest student-housing operator by number of beds, paid $52 million for the 780-bed Retreat at San Marcos, a new 187-cottage community developed by Landmark and serving Texas State University in San Marcos. Landmark also is developing more than 660 beds near the University of Mississippi in Oxford with EdR and about 590 beds for Pennsylvania State University in State College, Pa., which EdR has an option to buy. The projects, in which rents could be as much as $695 and $865 a month, respectively, should open next year.

On the University of Mississippi campus, single rooms on campus start at $5,600, or $622 a month, for the nine-month academic year, not including meals. At the Taylor Bend community a half-mile from campus, a three-bedroom, three-bath unit starts at $535 a person per month. Leases for off-campus housing typically are for 12 months.

Developers believe they will continue to attract students with amenities. At the Cottages of Columbia near the University of Missouri, the units include 9-foot ceilings, stainless-steel appliances, hardwood floors and marble bathroom countertops.

"My parents told me to not get used to living like this," said Avery Bond, a Louisiana State University senior who has lived at the Cottages of Baton Rouge for three years.

The Property Report
Dutch Firm's Plight Produces U.S. Deal --- Wereldhave, Facing Debt Payments, Is Selling Assets in Recovering Markets
By Maura Webber Sadovi
784 words
24 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The financial storm in Europe is presenting opportunities for investors in U.S. properties as hard-pressed real-estate companies and lenders sell assets.

Wereldhave NV, a Dutch real-estate company that has purchased office and residential properties in California, the Washington, D.C., area and Texas since 1978, has put its U.S. portfolio valued at roughly $800 million on the block to help pay down debt and shore up its operations in Europe.

"They're retreating to core areas," John Lutzius, a managing director with Green Street Advisors, said of Wereldhave. "The company's invested in many markets . . . and that's led them into areas beyond their expertise."

One of Wereldhave's first U.S. sales: a baseball-themed San Diego office building. The company is selling the 15-story DiamondView Tower overlooking the San Diego Padres' right field for $122 million to a venture of Cigna Corp. The price is 24% lower than Wereldhave paid in 2008. The deal is expected to close this week.

The deal highlights why strapped European companies searching for quick cash are looking to sell assets in the recovering U.S. markets. They aren't selling assets in companies in countries such as Spain and Italy, where prices are in the doldrums.

By contrast, values in the San Diego market have improved in the past year, although they aren't back to their 2008 levels. Cigna and a private San Diego real-estate company cofounded by Philip Monroe and Dennis Cruzan are expected to realize a return of just over 6% annually, according to people with knowledge of the property. That shows a strengthening of the market because buyers in the area generally have been looking for returns in the 7.8% range over the past 12 months, according to Real Capital Analytics.

Wereldhave is in a sales mode partly because its shares have been punished more by investors than other European real-estate companies. The FTSE EPRA/Nareit Global Real Estate Index of European companies has seen a roughly 20% increase this year.

But it has been a tough year for Wereldhave, whose Dutch name translates to world assets. Its shares have fallen about 11% year to date and the company disappointed investors during the summer when it revalued its U.S. and U.K. portfolios.

The revaluation was needed partly because initial bids for the San Diego property, which was put on the block early in the year, came in lower than expected. Also, the performance of some recently acquired U.K. shopping centers was disappointing and the sovereign-debt crisis has hurt U.K. consumer spending outside of London. "The rest of the U.K. is really suffering," said Charles Bloema, Wereldhave's corporate treasurer.

Wereldhave also saw a management shake-up this year in which Chief Executive Hans Par stepped down and was succeeded by Dirk Anbeek. In August, the company hired broker Eastdil Secured to market the U.S. properties in one or several larger bundles rather than on a one-off basis like the San Diego property. The company said it would use part of the proceeds to reduce debt to a loan-to-value ratio of about 40%, from 47%. Wereldhave earlier had said it would use the money to acquire European properties and invest in redeveloping properties.

Wereldhave executives now acknowledge the company paid too much for DiamondView. The deal closed in September 2008 during the market meltdown, and Wereldhave executives tried to compensate by demanding an $8 million price reduction, recalls Steven Black, president of Cisterra Development, which sold DiamondView. Cisterra dropped the price to $161.5 million. "We sold in the nick of time," Mr. Black said. "It's the best haircut I've ever received."

Built in 2007, DiamondView, overlooking Petco Park, is one of San Diego's newest and toniest office buildings. Office workers can watch games from balconies. A skybox that includes a bar and outdoor patios with 12-foot-long fire pits also can be rented for events. The building is about 96% occupied by such tenants as Cox Communications Inc. CBRE Group Inc. represented Wereldhave in the negotiations.

San Diego real-estate experts say DiamondView's new owner still faces challenges. The third-quarter vacancy rate for the city's downtown office market was 21%, above the 10.8% rate of one of its strongest suburban markets to the north, according to real-estate services firm Cushman & Wakefield.

Investors Spill Into Asia for Returns
By Fiona Law
725 words
23 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
19
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A flood of cash into Asian investments has pushed stocks, currencies and real estate markets sharply higher, leaving governments struggling to stem the inflows for fear they will fuel inflation.

The inflow is a result of aggressive stimulus measures by central banks including the U.S. Federal Reserve, which in September announced its latest round of bond buying to help keep U.S. yields low in hopes of sparking growth. Low rates have in turn sent money managers scrambling to all corners of Asia seeking better investment returns.

Indonesia, for one, had a net inflow of about $1.3 billion in bonds last month, compared with a net outflow of $540 million in August, according to Standard Chartered PLC. South Korea also witnessed net inflows of $1.4 billion in September, versus $2.4 billion in outflows the month before, the bank said in a recent report.

Standard Chartered said in the report that it expects investment inflows to Asia to accelerate "if Chinas economic data continues to stabilize and the Chinese yuan establishes a strengthening trend."

Some of the region's smallest equity markets have been big beneficiaries, with Thailand's share index up 28% this year, the Philippines 24% higher and a main Indian index rallying 23%. Hong Kong reached a 14-month high on Monday. Only mainland China is lower this year as concerns linger over the health over its giant economy.

Hong Kong and Singapore, two of the most-developed economies in the region, have also received overseas cash that has pumped up not just their currencies and stocks but also their housing markets.

Home prices in Hong Kong have surged to records, after doubling over the past four years, leading Financial Secretary John Tsang to warn Sunday that the real estate market has "seriously disconnected" with the slowing economy, and he threatened to impose measures to curb property prices.

In Thailand, the central bank set new rules on Monday to encourage capital outflows and to balance the heavy influx of money. It did so by loosening restrictions for listed firms and individuals to invest in overseas equities and foreign-currency-denominated domestic bonds.

Singapore's policy makers are also concerned about high property prices as private-home prices jumped 56% last quarter. Both Hong Kong and Singapore have stepped up efforts in the past few weeks to tighten rules on home loans to try to cool off the market.

"Given the relatively small and open economies, Singapore and Hong Kong are more exposed to loose monetary policy in the U.S.," said Andrew Colquhoun, Fitch Ratings' head of Asian-Pacific sovereign ratings. "That could possibly increase their risks of more volatile asset prices and inflationary pressure."

In currency markets, the Singapore dollar has jumped almost 6% this year against the U.S. dollar, making it Asia's strongest performer. And in Hong Kong, the de facto central bank stepped into the currency markets on Friday to weaken the tightly controlled Hong Kong dollar for the first time in almost three years after it had climbed to the top of its trading band.

Other countries in Southeast Asia are also feeling the wave of liquidity in the global economy. The flows are also seen in foreign direct investment, with Indonesia recording a 22% increase in FDI to a record high of 56.6 trillion rupiah ($5.9 billion) last quarter.

Meanwhile, the region's bond market has recorded unprecedented issuance of as much as $158 billion this year, up from $112.7 billion in all of last year, according to data provider Dealogic.

Some economists worry that all the money sloshing around will start to push up consumer prices in the region, and that will lead to interest-rate increases and start to choke off economic growth.

"Inflation is going to snap back next year," said Frederic Neumann, co-head of Asian economic research at HSBC Holdings PLC. "As the region's economy [picks up its growth momentum], with the global liquidity, increased wages and prices, inflation will be the No. 1 risk in the region."

Indonesia, Thailand, the Philippines and Malaysia are more prone to inflation, while China, with restricted capital inflows, will be less impacted in terms of prices, Mr. Neumann said.

Global Finance: Analyst Is Walking the Talk --- Former Housing Strategist at Morgan Stanley Forms Buy-to-Rent Business
By Jeannette Neumann
810 words
22 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Oliver Chang is one Wall Street analyst who took his own advice.

As the head of U.S. housing strategy at Morgan Stanley, Mr. Chang wrote a six-page research report in July 2011 about how the fallout from the real-estate crash was spurring demand for home rentals.

The report, "A Rentership Society," achieved must-read status among investors in the growing strategy of snapping up foreclosed homes and then renting them. As foreclosures exploded after the financial crisis, investors started to amass more and more houses. His report took the strategy to a broader audience of institutional investors.

"That was something many investors hadn't thought about before he brought it to the market," said Ron Mass, former head of structured products at Western Asset Management, which oversees around $447 billion. He is looking at single-family real estate opportunities.

In May, Mr. Chang left his job on Wall Street. In August, he opened Sylvan Road Capital LLC to buy, renovate and rent previously foreclosed homes. He has a partnership with Delmar Realty Advisors, Atlanta, which has been in the buy-to-rent business since 2008.

Sylvan is spending the first $20 million of a commitment from private-equity giant Carlyle Group LP, with the potential for additional funds that could take the amount to $300 million with debt-financing, people familiar with the matter said. Sylvan plans to spend more than $1 billion buying homes over the next two years.

Sylvan owns about 200 homes and spends an average of $30,000 to $50,000 to renovate them, Mr. Chang said.

"This is not about going long home prices or the housing recovery," Mr. Chang said. "We are taking an existing market . . . and taking a portion of that and making that institutionally owned and managed."

The influence of Mr. Chang's reports and the investment from a major private-equity firm have given his firm added buzz in the suddenly crowded field of real-estate investors with high hopes for the rental income the properties will produce -- and the returns the eventual sale of the homes could bring.

Rich Ford, a managing director in Jefferies Group Inc.'s real-estate investment banking group, estimated that institutional investors have raised about $8 billion primarily in equity to buy homes to rent within the past year. At $100,000 a home, that would buy 80,000 homes, he said.

Daniel Bathon Jr., CEO of VineBrook Partners LLC, a Lexington, Mass., investment firm that owns about 300 homes, said Mr. Chang's launch means other funds "sitting on the fence are now going to start thinking: 'I have to have a piece of this strategy somewhere in my portfolio.'" Mr. Chang also has skeptics who wonder if his prowess as an analyst will translate into returns for investors. "It's like an equity analyst reviewing General Electric," said Thomas Silvia, Vinebrook's chief financial officer. "They can be a good analyst, but can they be the CEO of General Electric?"

Mr. Chang responds that Sylvan has "a very complex business model that involves a significant amount of operating expertise, and that's exactly why I decided to work with the partners that I did."

Analysts at John Burns Real Estate Consulting, Zelman & Associates and Amherst Securities Group were writing about the investment opportunity before or around the same time as Mr. Chang. Some major investors were considering the strategy before he sang its praises. Pacific Investment Management Co., a unit of Allianz SE, for instance, was considering buy-to-rent investments in early 2011.

At Morgan Stanley, Mr. Chang spoke with many buy-to-rent investors. In late 2010, Mr. Chang spoke with James McClelland, president and CEO of Mack Cos., about the firm's property management and data. Mack has been buying and renting homes since 1998 and now owns and manages around 1,000 of them.

But not everyone finds the strategy so compelling. Scott Simon, Pimco's head of mortgage- and asset-backed securities teams, said the fund ultimately decided against major buy-to-rent investments. As more investors have piled in, home prices have increased in some markets, eating into profits. "We don't think it's a bad trade," Mr. Simon said. "But it's just not the panacea that everyone thinks."

Mr. Chang said he thinks the rental business will develop much like apartment rentals have in recent decades, with demand for both large and small managers. "There will always be a significant portion of the country that rents, and given the current economic environment in the country, that proportion is growing," Mr. Chang said.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Mortgage REITs: Not Just Yet
By Ben Levisohn
711 words
20 October 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The housing market is in recovery mode, but one type of mortgage investment has gotten hammered lately. Experts say investors should wait awhile longer before wading back in.

Mortgage real-estate investment trusts are companies that buy mortgage-backed securities and pay most of the returns to investors as dividends. The group plunged 5.9% in the five days through Oct. 15, the largest such drop since October 2011.

The selloff might have some investors considering picking up the REITs on the cheap. In 2010 and 2011, similar drops have presented opportunities for people with the patience -- and nerves -- to jump back in. This time, however, investors should resist the urge, experts say.

Past selloffs were driven mainly by fears about the sustainability of the financial system. The current plunge has more to do with concerns about the sustainability of the sector's dividends, spurred by the Federal Reserve's decision to start buying mortgage-backed securities, and experts say the worries are unlikely to be assuaged soon.

"There's more room to go," says Andy Kapyrin, director of research at RegentAtlantic Capital in Morristown, N.J.

Mortgage REITs raise money through initial public offerings and load up on mortgage-backed securities. They then borrow more money and buy more bonds, until they are able to pay sizable dividends, often 10% or more.

That has been successful in the past because mortgage REITs typically pay far less to borrow money than the yields they receive from the bonds they own. (The difference between the two is known as the spread.)

Mortgage REITs dropped 8.1% during the five days ended Oct. 4, 2011, because the choppy financial markets made investors question whether the companies would be able to get the short-term financing they need to finance their positions.

The ability to make a profit off their borrow-short-term, lend-long-term strategy, however, was never called into question -- as long as the euro zone didn't fall to pieces. It didn't, and the REITs returned 40% during the following 12 months.

Now the focus is on the other side of the spread -- the yield mortgage REITs earn on their purchases. The Federal Reserve's low-rate policy has pushed the yields on all bonds lower, including those of mortgage-backed securities. Since January, the yield on the Barclays U.S. MBS Conventional 30 Year Index has dropped to 2.4%, from 2.9%. "The spread between yields and funding is abnormally low," says Sean Kelleher, president of Shay Asset Management in Miami, which oversees about $700 million.

Ultralow mortgage rates have many homeowners rushing to refinance their mortgages. On Oct. 3, the Mortgage Bankers Association said that refinance activity had hit its highest level since April 2009.

That's bad news for mortgage REITs. Every time one of their mortgages refinances they have to take the money and reinvest at lower interest rates, putting further pressure on the spread, says Jason Brady, portfolio manager of the $11.5 billion Thornburg Investment Income Builder Fund.

Experts are most worried about REITs that purchase pools of loans backed by government agencies like Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. That would include the likes of Annaly Capital Management, whose shares have dropped 6.7% during the past month, and American Agency Capital, which has dropped 9.2%.

REITs that purchase private mortgage pools, including Invesco Mortgage Capital, might hold up better because the securities they buy are less affected by Fed purchases because the central bank is only purchasing mortgage-backed securities issued by the U.S. agencies, says Thornburg's Mr. Brady.

Like most investments, diversification could help reduce risk. One possibility: the iShares FTSE NAREIT Mortgage Plus Capped Index exchange-traded fund, which has a yield of 14.2%, after having dropped 4.3% over the past month.

But the chances of continued falling prices are considerable. "While the dividends can be quite attractive, mortgage REITs can be volatile," says Jay Wong, manager of the $175 million Payden Value Leaders Fund. He adds: "They're not something we usually invest in."
