The unusual activity followed brief price spikes Tuesday afternoon in seven oil-related stocks that led to reviews by exchanges and confusion on trading desks. Tuesday's swings were caused by a trader accidentally entering the wrong size for an order to trade a basket of stocks, according to a person familiar with the matter. The exchanges on Tuesday said the trades would stand.

Questcor Pharmaceuticals plunged 24.17, or 48%, to 26.35, after Aetna limited reimbursement for the pharmaceutical drug maker's primary product, which treats infantile spasms.

Facebook jumped 1.42, or 6.5%, to 23.29, the second-biggest daily increase since its May initial public offering. The advanced trimmed the shares' loss the last three months to 27%.

U.S. News: Green Shoots Sprout in Desert
By Dawn Wotapka
917 words
20 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
7
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

HENDERSON, Nev. -- The latest sign that the housing market is bubbling to life: The artificial waterfall at the entryway to Lake Las Vegas is again flowing.

Few developments were hit harder in the real-estate crash than this mixed-use project in the desert 20 miles southeast of Las Vegas. Anchored by a 320-acre man-made lake, it counts singer Celine Dion among the well-heeled owners, but saw home values plunge, sales stall and foreclosures rise.

The development's casino and a Ritz-Carlton hotel closed in 2010, and the company that owned much of the undeveloped land filed for bankruptcy protection. The waterfall remained dry, much to the chagrin of buyers of the project's roughly 1,700 homes.

Today foreclosures are down, the casino and hotel have new operators and freshly-built and existing homes are selling, albeit at much lower prices. Hedge-fund manager John Paulson's Real Estate Private Equity Group recently snapped up 530 acres of developable land in Lake Las Vegas for $17 million in cash from lenders including Credit Suisse, for four cents on the dollar.

The signs of a recovery in Lake Las Vegas show that the housing-market improvements that first appeared this year in many city centers and core neighborhoods are beginning to be seen in outlying areas. They also indicate that some of the hardest-hit spots have gone through a purging process and seen prices reset at market levels.

"Two years ago, no one would have touched anything to do with Lake Las Vegas," says Andrew Stegen, general manager of Ravella, the hotel that filled the Ritz-Carlton's shell in early 2011. The Paulson purchase "is a huge boost of confidence."

The Paulson firm made billions by betting against the housing market during the boom years. It has been bullish on the broader market's long-term prospects since first investing in land at the end of 2009, says Michael Barr, a Paulson portfolio manager.

To be sure, no one is calling the Lake Las Vegas development booming. Two of the three golf courses, designed by golf legends Jack Nicklaus and Tom Weiskopf, are closed and their brown grass has been an eyesore and a turnoff to some potential buyers, local real-estate agents and builders say.

Overall, Lake Las Vegas home values have tumbled dramatically from their peak and have barely improved since they hit bottom, according to real-estate website Zillow Inc. In a recent deal, a three-bedroom, three-bath home sold for $540,000, down from the $1 million it would have gone for at the 2006 peak says Robyn Yates, owner of Windermere Prestige Properties.

Still, just 24 foreclosure notices were filed in July, an 80% plunge from a year ago, according to RealtyTrac. Indeed, during a visit earlier this summer, business was brisk at a Ravella hotel bar which has Tuscan-inspired views of manicured hedges surrounding a fire pit, with customers sipping martinis and noshing on pappardelle with veal meatballs.

"It's fun watching the area come back to life," says Berny Dohrmann, chairman of CEO Space International, a networking group for small-business owners that began holding conferences at Lake Las Vegas in 2009. Back then, numerous shops were closed and the area seemed like a "dying Western ghost town," Mr. Dohrmann says.

Nearly four years ago, Mike Howard and a partner purchased a foreclosed three-bedroom home in a gated section of Lake Las Vegas. Back then, the area seemed depressed and there were rumors the lake would dry up, recalls the 71-year-old retired chemistry teacher and athletic coach.

Today, he's optimistic about the area's future. "We're not seeing prices jumping, but we feel comfortable that we've hit bottom," he says.

Lake Las Vegas was developed by a group including Texas billionaire brothers Sid and Lee Bass and the late developer Ron Boeddeker.

While overbuilding caused Las Vegas to collapse during the housing crash, Lake Las Vegas was hit even harder. At the height of the city's foreclosure crisis, one in every 45 homes received a foreclosure filing, compared with one in every 13 in Lake Las Vegas.

In 2008, the developers defaulted on a $540 million loan arranged by Credit Suisse.

The project spent about two years in bankruptcy before emerging in the summer of 2010, with most of it going back to creditors. Deutsche Bank, which declined to comment, now owns the former Ritz-Carlton.

More than 50 homes changed hands in the second quarter, up from 43 in the first quarter. New tenants include a coffee shop, two restaurants and a photography studio, says broker Julie A. Barbagallo.

At Ravella, which is operated by Dolce Hotels and Resorts, second-quarter occupancy climbed 13% from a year ago, while revenue per available room, a key revenue measure for hotels, increased 40% and the hotel is close to being profitable, according to Mr. Stegen.

These days, the closed courses are the biggest reminder of the area's turmoil. Robb Beville, president of builder Harmony Homes, would like to see them reopen.

Mr. Beville has sold 13 homes his year, down from 14 last year, but he thinks sales would be higher if the courses were open and home buyers could enjoy views of lush greens and fairways.

Lake Las Vegas "still has its challenges," he says.

U.S. News: U.S. Housing Market Gives New Support To Economy
By Sarah Portlock and Alan Zibel
361 words
20 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal Europe
WSJE
7
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

WASHINGTON -- Sales of previously occupied homes and construction of single-family homes last month reached the highest level in more than two years as the turnaround of the long-troubled sector provides a boost to the tepid economic recovery.

Existing-home sales increased 7.8% in August from a month earlier to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.82 million, the National Association of Realtors said Wednesday. The month's sales were 9.3% above the same month a year earlier and are the highest since May 2010 when first-time homebuyers were rushing to qualify for a tax credit.

Meanwhile, housing starts increased 2.3% last month from July to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 750,000, according to a separate Commerce Department report Wednesday. Single-family home construction, which made up 71% of housing starts last month, was up to its highest levels since April 2010.

"The U.S. housing recovery is for real," said Sal Guatieri, senior economist with BMO Capital Markets.

The median sales price for previously owned homes in August was $187,400, up 9.5% from the same month a year earlier. The trend reflects a combination in the mix of homes, with more normal transactions beginning to occur and fewer distressed property sales, said Realtors' chief economist, Lawrence Yun.

"This is a surprisingly strong report," said Millan Mulraine, an economist with TD Securities. "The report reinforces our belief that the pieces for a more sustainable housing sector recovery are now falling into place."

The Commerce Department data showed single-family construction reached the highest level in more than two years, confirming that the sector is helping the economy.

Still, there are plenty of reasons for caution: Sales and construction starts are still below even their pre-bubble levels, and the year-over-year comparisons look strong because last year was terrible. Tax credits fueled a burst of sales activity in late 2009 and early 2010, and the hangover hit housing markets hard in much of 2011.

Positive signs from the housing market and action from Japan's central bank put U.S. stocks on track to post their sixth gain in the past seven trading days.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 46 points, or 0.4%, to 13611, in early-afternoon trading Wednesday.

The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index added three points, or 0.2%, to 1463. Consumer-discretionary and materials shares advanced, while the energy sector lagged behind. The Nasdaq Composite Index gained two points, or 0.1%, to 3180.

"There are many reasons to feel good about housing, including the data that we saw today," said Elizabeth Ptacek, real-estate analyst at Cleveland-based KeyBank. "There's not much positive out there and housing represents a bright spot."

Sales of previously occupied homes in August climbed more than expected from a month earlier, according to a National Association of Realtors report. While August housing starts rose less than economists had expected in August, construction of single-family homes rose to the highest level in more than two years, the Commerce Department said. New starts on apartment buildings saw a decline, but new building permits fell less than economists had projected.

The Bank of Japan increased the size of its asset-purchase program to 80 trillion yen ($1.01 trillion) from 70 trillion yen and extended the deadline of the program by six months to the end of 2013.

"The U.S. is in a slow and low-growth mode, but if we get some of the headwind lifted from Asia and Europe, then we're talking about an acceleration of the economy," said Diane Jaffee, senior portfolio manager at TCW Group.

Gold rose 0.1%, to $1,772.50 a troy ounce. The dollar fell against the euro and yen.

In corporate news, Cracker Barrel Old Country Store climbed 6.9% after reporting better-than-expected earnings and sales for the latest quarter and raising its dividend.

General Mills gained 2.2% after reporting fiscal first-quarter earnings that exceeded expectations.

Corning added 2.4% after Goldman Sachs analysts raised their rating on the shares to "buy," citing the company's efforts to diversify its business into other areas such as fiber-optic cable.

European Stocks

The Stoxx Europe 600 index rose 0.4% to 274.90, snapping a two-day losing streak, largely drawing on the same catalysts as in the U.S.

London's FTSE 100 index gained 0.4% to 5888.48, supported by bank shares. Germany's DAX 30 index rose 0.6% to 7390.76 and France's CAC 40 index gained 0.5% to 3531.82.

"We have had a good run-up in equities and we're in a waiting position from here," said Peter Garnry, equity strategist at Saxo Bank.

Among individual stocks, Heineken posted one of the biggest gains in the pan-European index, rallying 6.4%. The Dutch brewer got closer to securing a deal to gain control of Asia Pacific Breweries and its Tiger beer brand.

In London, HSBC Holdings and Lloyds Banking Group both added 1.7%.

World Currency Trading: Euro, Yen Rebound Against the Dollar
By William L. Watts and Deborah Levine
431 words
20 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
27
English
(c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

The euro turned back up against the dollar on Wednesday after a pair of reports on the U.S. housing sector showed some improvement.

The dollar had been up earlier as the Bank of Japan's expanded asset-purchase program showed more central banks than just the Federal Reserve are injecting further stimulus into the economy.

But the greenback gave up gains after the National Association of Realtors said existing home sales rose at a faster rate in August than analysts expected. An earlier report showed housing starts last month rose less than expected.

At midday in New York, the euro climbed to $1.3068 from $1.3048 late Tuesday. The dollar traded at 78.42 yen from 78.82 yen, while the euro was at 102.49 yen versus 102.80 yen. The pound bought $1.6214 from $1.6245; the dollar fetched 0.9269 Swiss franc from 0.9285 franc.

During European hours, the euro declined as German officials reportedly sought to water down proposals for a European banking union and a supervisory role for the European Central Bank.

Proposals for a strong banking union are seen by some "as a critical foundation for a sound currency," said Boris Schlossberg, managing director at BK Asset Management. "Therefore, any proposals to diminish the ECB's supervisory authority is viewed very negatively by the market."

The yen mostly dropped after the Bank of Japan moved to further ease monetary policy by expanding its own asset-purchase program -- a move many didn't expect so soon. The central bank's move bolstered global equities, undercutting demand for such haven currencies as Japan's yen and the U.S. dollar.

The Bank of Japan's decision comes less than a week after the Federal Reserve announced it would embark on a third round of quantitative easing to underpin the U.S. recovery.

"Investors had been aware of the possibility of such a move but it had not been fully priced into the market. Consequently, the yen took a hit when the BOJ made its announcement," noted strategists at Moneycorp in London.

The move helped revive a risk rally that had been fueled in part by last week's Fed move and the ECB's announcement earlier this month of a bond-buying plan.

The WSJ dollar index, which tracks the greenback against a slightly larger basket, slipped to 69.18 from 69.30.

The Property Report
Real-Estate Firms Dip Toes in IPO Pool --- After Slow Start This Year, Improving Outlook in Housing and Other Sectors Prompts Companies to Consider Offerings
By Telis Demos and A.D. Pruitt
886 words
19 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

The market for initial public offerings by real-estate companies has been grim this year, but that might be about to change.

Some companies are moving to capitalize on the hottest real-estate sectors, as well as the early signs that the housing market, at long last, may be poised for a rebound.

On Wednesday, Spirit Realty Finance Inc., a company that leases property to retail chains like Shopko Stores, AMC Theatres and Applebee's, hopes to raise as much as $487 million in an IPO. It would be the largest IPO of a real-estate investment trust, or REIT, since May 2011, according to Dealogic.

The parent of Realogy Corp., which owns Century 21, Coldwell Banker and other leading residential brands, wants to raise as much as $1 billion in an IPO and could launch its deal over the next few weeks, according to people familiar with its plans.

Meanwhile, in what would be one of the largest real-estate IPOs, Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc. has filed with regulators to take apartment giant Archstone public. Apartment buildings have been one of the hottest property types in the past two years thanks to strong demand for rentals.

"These deals would be an encouraging sign for the real-estate equity capital markets and also a sign that real-estate fundamentals continue to improve," said Jay Leupp, a realty income portfolio manager at Lazard Asset Management LLC.

The weakness of the real-estate IPO market has put a damper on plans by a wide range of companies. Dozens of privately held real-estate companies and REITs that aren't traded have been longing for a stronger IPO market as an exit strategy.

Shares of REITs, which pay at least 90% of their income in dividends, have had a strong year along with the rest of the stock market. But 2012 has been one of the slowest years in the past decade for REIT public offerings, with only 2008 seeing fewer, according to Dealogic.

The reason: Wall Street has been concerned that the new REITs won't be able to maintain earnings growth because of the weak economy. As a result, investors have demanded discounts to asset value from the untested companies that they haven't been willing to give, according to Mr. Leupp.

"Investors are certainly hungry for yield, but yield that is safe from inflation through earnings growth," he says.

But now, improving prices and sales volumes in some real-estate markets, plus continued efforts to keep borrowing rates low by the Federal Reserve, are leading companies seeking IPOs and their bankers to pitch the companies as early bets on a coming turnaround.

There also has been a strong demand for REITs by fund managers. Real-estate-focused equity funds saw the largest increase of any fund category in July, with $800 million in new assets, according to the most recent data from Lipper, which tracks fund flows.

Spirit is aiming to be in a position to grow by deleveraging and adding new retail properties, according to the company's prospectus. The company generates most of its revenue by leasing properties to retail chains and fast food restaurants. About 30% of its lease income comes from Shopko Stores, a discount general merchandise chain outside of Green Bay, Wisc.

Joel Beam, fund manager of the Forward Select Income Fund, estimates that Spirit would yield 7.4% at the midpoint of its expected IPO price range, an attractive return in the current low interest-rate environment.

Other companies believe they can build a strong growth story on the turnaround of the home-sales market. Realogy's parent, Domus Holdings, for example, may be very close to launching its $1 billion IPO, according to people familiar with its deal. The IPO is likely to launch before November, these people said.

A spokesman for Domus declined to comment.

Realogy was bought by private-equity firm Apollo in 2007 for $8.5 billion. The value of Realogy declined sharply during the downturn.

Potentially the largest new listing would be Archstone, which has filed with regulators to go public as a REIT. It owns more than 400 apartment complexes in the U.S. and Europe, primarily around major cities. The company filed initial plans to raise just $100 million, but it may ultimately seek to raise as much as $2 billion to $3 billion in an IPO, people familiar with its plans said. A Kehman spokeswoman declined to comment.

Archstone and Lehman are hoping an IPO would catch the crest of the wave in the rental-apartment market that has pushed values of prime property in the hot regions to record highs. Archstone, if it went forward with the IPO, also would likely see demand because it is one of the largest and most established players in the market.

"Archstone is an enormous portfolio of institutional grade apartment buildings being managed by somebody that's already successful in the REIT space," said Jeung Hyun, a fund manager at Adelante Capital Management.

The Property Report
Hotel Construction Picks Up --- Increase in Occupancy and Room Rates Fuels Rebound; Lenders More Willing
By Kris Hudson
932 words
19 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Hotel construction, which dropped sharply during the economic downturn, has staged a modest turnaround, buoyed by building booms in New York as well as in small towns crowded with workers drilling oil-shale formations.

In the past four quarters, construction starts for U.S. hotels are up 32% by room count from the same period a year ago, and new-project announcements are up 22%, according to Lodging Econometrics, which tracks the hotel industry globally. Fueling the construction rebound are rising occupancy and room rates at U.S. hotels since 2009 and a willingness by more lenders to make hotel-construction loans, typically the most volatile and risky of commercial real-estate classes.

The average U.S. room rate of $105.53 in the first seven months of this year marks a 6.6% increase from the same period in 2009, according to Smith Travel Research. Average occupancy increased in the same span by nearly seven percentage points to 62.3%.

The pickup in hotel construction is a mixed signal for the industry. Although it is viewed as a sign that developers are confident about the economy's prospects and about consumers' willingness to travel, some owners of existing hotels worry that too many new hotels can lead to lower room rates in the future.

But so far, analysts say the new construction cycle isn't likely to lead to an oversupply of rooms, coming after nearly three years of declines in construction. At the end of the second quarter, developers had 474 hotels, totaling 60,000 rooms, under construction in the U.S. That is up from 437 hotels and nearly 55,000 rooms in the previous year. New-project announcements in the past year totaled 1,180 hotels, up from 926 announced in the previous year.

"Very modestly and very quietly, a new [construction] cycle has begun," said Patrick Ford, president of Lodging Econometrics. The company predicts a steady rise in new hotel openings through 2014.

New York remains by far the most-active market for hotel construction with 7,248 rooms being built, amounting to roughly 11% of the national total. Though the city's room count grew 7% in 2010 and 2.3% last year, its occupancy has remained above 80% since 2010. "New development is not catching up with the increase in demand," said Issac Hera, chief executive of Brack Capital Real Estate USA, which is developing three hotels in Manhattan.

A similar hotel boom is unfolding in a far different locale: rural communities atop oil-shale formations being drilled for oil and natural gas. Of the top 10 U.S. markets for hotel construction in the past year, three are in oil-shale areas: North Dakota, with 2,088 rooms under construction; the greater Corpus Christi area in Texas, with 1,491 rooms; and rural Oklahoma, with 1,242 rooms.

Most developers in these smaller markets are building budget and midscale hotels to accommodate oil-shale workers and other energy-industry employee. The hotels are needed in these areas to house the many workers, from engineers to rig workers, needed to drill and complete wells across the region. Since those workforces move around often to different drill sites, companies prefer to house them in hotels rather than longer-term residences such as houses or apartments.

Braxton Development, based in Bozeman, Mont., built Microtel Inns in the small North Dakota towns of Williston and Dickinson last year and opened a Hampton Inn in Williston last August. "When we first started, we were the second hotel in Williston to get built," Braxton principal Jon Braxton said. "But more competition came along quickly. It's as crazy as ever. Hotels still are running fairly full."

However, some hoteliers worry that the hotel-construction booms in markets like North Dakota amount to a bubble set to pop, especially if federal regulation cools the drilling industry or if gas and oil prices decline sharply. Lamont Cos., based in Aberdeen, S.D., recently built a Hampton Inn in Dickinson, N.D., and the builder also is planning a Candlewood Suites in the town. Yet Jeff Lamont, president and chief executive, said he would sell the properties if the right offer surfaces. "I know of six or seven hotels going up in Dickinson," he said. "It has us a little nervous about what might happen with the occupancy once all of these hotels are built. To me, it seems like there might not be enough demand there for all of them."

Overall, two-thirds of new hotels under construction in the U.S. are either upscale or upper-midscale, according to Lodging Econometrics. Those categories include brands such as Hilton Garden Inn, Courtyard by Marriott and Holiday Inn. And many of those are limited-service properties, meaning they don't include restaurants, spas or other amenities found in full-service hotels.

The construction boom is made possible by the availability of construction loans, which dried up during the economic downturn but are now starting to flow again.

Joe Hoesley, vice president of commercial real estate for U.S. Bancorp in Minneapolis, said underwriting criteria remains fairly stringent for hotel developments. Most banks refuse to issue loans covering more than 50% to 60% of a project's cost, and interest rates on those loans still are higher than loans to build offices or apartments, he said.

Stocks Inch Up Amid Lowest Volume in 2012
By Jonathan Cheng And Kaitlyn Kiernan
814 words
19 September 2012
16:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.S. stocks finished a touch higher as investors calibrated two positive readings on the U.S. economy against hopes for more help from the Federal Reserve.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average edged up 4.49 points, or 0.03%, to 13107.48 for its first gain in three days. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index added 1.19 points, or 0.08%, to 1410.49, while the Nasdaq Composite rose 4.05 points, or 0.13%, to 3081.19.

With two days remaining in the month, the blue-chip Dow has added 0.8% so far in August, while the S&P 500 has tacked on 2.3%.

Leading the day's gains were telecommunications and consumer discretionary stocks. Verizon Communications led the Dow components, while energy and utilities shares weighed. Coca-Cola was the biggest decliner on the Dow.

The modest gains came after a pair of readings on the U.S. economy came in better than expected.

A revision to U.S. gross domestic product in the second quarter showed growth at 1.7% from the previous quarter, higher than an original reading of 1.5% and in-line with economists' expectations. The stronger reading reflects higher consumer spending and fewer imports.

Separately, pending home sales for July increased 2.4% in July after declining 1.4% in June. The rise exceeded the 1% gain expected by analysts and pushed the number of pending home sales to the highest since April 2010.

The Federal Reserve also released its "beige book" report on economic conditions around the country, which saw modest and moderate growth across the U.S. Most districts saw steady or "slightly" growing employment. That report jibed with expectations that the Fed could soon act to boost the economy, with more clarity to come as soon as Friday, when Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke speaks at a meeting in Jackson Hole, Wyo.

"It's clear to me that while the stimulus program may not have as much of an economic impact, the anticipation for what these programs might be is significant... Every time Bernanke has hinted, the market has a positive reaction," says Omar Aguilar, chief investment officer of equities at Charles Schwab Investment Management.

However, after a summer rally driven by what Mr. Aguilar believes are broad expectations for more easing, "the expectations are so high relative to what's happening that we're probably set for a disappointment," he says.

European markets pared early losses to finish the day down slightly, as comments from European Central Bank President Mario Draghi boosted hopes that the ECB will act to stabilize the borrowing costs of the euro-zone's frail economies. The Stoxx Europe 600 ended the day down 0.1%.

Asian markets were mixed. The Shanghai Composite fell 1% to close at a fresh 3½-year low, and Japan's Nikkei Stock Average rose 0.4%. China's Xinhua News Agency reported that China's top economic planner said a rebound in housing prices would need to be prevented to curb speculative demand.

Crude-oil futures fell 0.9% to $95.49 a barrel after the Group of Seven leading industrialized economies called for a lift in oil production in the face of fears about Hurricane Isaac and tensions in Iran. Gold futures slipped 0.4% to $1,659.80 an ounce. The dollar fell against the euro but rose slightly against the yen. Demand for Treasurys fell, pushing the yield on the 10-year note higher to 1.652%.

In corporate news, shares of H.J. Heinz fell after the ketchup maker reported better-than-expected fiscal first-quarter earnings but its revenue fell short of forecasts.

Yelp jumped even as 52.7 million shares became available for sale, marking the end of a 180-day lockup period on stock sales by company insiders following the online review site's initial public offering. That is an unusual move for the end of a lockup period, but its shares had plunged 31% over the previous three weeks.

WellPoint climbed after the health insurer said Chief Executive Angela Braly resigned Tuesday. The company appointed its general counsel, John Cannon, as interim CEO while it searches for a permanent successor. A disappointing second-quarter earnings report had sharpened concerns about the company's strategy.

Jos. A. Bank Clothiers surged after the men's apparel retailer reported better-than-expected earnings and revenue, citing strength in direct-marketing sales.

Zale gained after the jewelry retailer reported a narrower-than-expected fiscal fourth-quarter loss and said it expected to return to profitability in the current fiscal year.

Tomi Kilgore contributed to this article.

Canadian Dollar Flat Vs US Dollar
By David George-Cosh

19 September 2012

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

TORONTO--The Canadian dollar ended trading flat against the U.S. dollar for the third-straight session Wednesday, but underperformed most of its other major currency peers in the aftermath of the Bank of Japan's surprise announcement that it would expand its asset purchase program.

The U.S. dollar was recently at C$0.9748, unchanged from 8:00 a.m. EDT Wednesday and C$0.9742 late Tuesday, according to data provider CQG. The U.S. dollar moved as low as C$0.9724 during overnight trading, but later retraced its losses.

The Canadian dollar underperformed most of its G10 currency peers against the U.S. dollar, with the euro, Australian dollar and Swiss franc outperforming after a pair of reports on the U.S. housing sector showed signs of a slow recovery.

The Japanese yen quickly erased losses made during the overnight trading as investors unwound long positions held in favor of the Canadian dollar and U.S. dollar after the Bank of Japan made the surprise announcement it would add Y10 trillion to its asset purchasing program.

U.S. housing starts rose 2.3% in August, slightly under expectations but still moving to its highest level in two years, while existing home sales increased by 7.8% last month.

Meanwhile, Canadian housing prices increased by 0.2% in August from July, the smallest increase for the month of August in 12 years, according to the Teranet-National Bank National Composite House Price Index.

The positive U.S. data should ultimately bode well for the Canadian economy, keeping the Canadian dollar "in check," said Firas Askari, head of foreign exchange at BMO Capital Markets in Toronto.

"There has seem to be some signs of traction in the U.S. housing market, which has been very good for Canadian manufacturers," Mr. Askari said. "Their houses are built in the U.S. but a lot of their raw materials come from over here."

Traders continue to expect the Canadian dollar to trade within current ranges for the rest of the week.

The only major domestic data release is consumer price index figures on Friday and even that doesn't appear likely to do much to influence the loonie, said Dave Bradley, director of foreign exchange at Scotiabank Global Banking and Markets in Toronto.

"The Canadian dollar hasn't reacted that much to previous releases," he said. "At the end of the day, it's been doing its own thing."

Markets
Yen Rebounds on Dollar After BOJ Moves
By Nicole Hong

16:29
The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

NEW YORK—The dollar fell against the yen after the Bank of Japan expanded its monetary easing program to stimulate the local economy and weaken the yen's persistent appreciation.

Japan's central bank announced it would increase the size of its asset-purchase program to ¥80 trillion ($1.01 trillion) from ¥70 trillion and extend the program's deadline until the end of December 2013.

The dollar strengthened against the yen in an initial knee-jerk reaction as monetary easing generally weakens the local currency. However, the yen climbed in the New York session as traders realized the program "wasn't all that impressive in terms of what the BOJ actually delivered," said Ron Leven, currency strategist at Morgan Stanley in New York.

In late-afternoon trade, the dollar fell to ¥78.38 from ¥78.82 late Tuesday. It had risen as high as ¥79.23 earlier in the session. The euro also dropped against the yen to ¥102.28 from ¥102.81.

The Federal Reserve and European Central Bank both launched similar programs earlier this month. Last week, the Fed announced open-ended buying of mortgage-backed securities to jump-start the U.S. economy while the ECB pledged on Sept. 6 to buy bonds of struggling euro-zone countries if they requested aid.

"In the wake of the bold initiatives from the Federal Reserve and European Central Bank, what the Bank of Japan is proposing seems pretty modest," Mr. Leven said.

Another force supporting the Japanese currency was yen-buying from Japanese firms as they looked to hedge their balance sheets ahead of the third-quarter's end on Sept. 30, said Greg Anderson, a currency strategist at Citigroup in New York. The yen's decline after the initial BOJ statement gave corporations a cheaper entry into the yen, "close enough to their budget level that they didn't want to let it get past them," Mr. Anderson added.

Meanwhile, the euro rose against the dollar after better-than-expected U.S. housing data pointed to a more sustained upturn in the struggling U.S. housing market.

Sales of existing homes rose 7.8% in August to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 4.82 million, according to the National Association of Realtors. Economists polled by Dow Jones Newswires had expected a 2% rise to 4.56 million. U.S. housing starts also increased 2.3% in August, as single-family home construction jumped to its highest level since April 2010.

The euro recently traded at $1.3060, up from $1.3048 late Tuesday. The pound bought $1.6214 compared with $1.6245, and the dollar fetched 0.9275 Swiss franc from 0.9285 franc.

The Australian dollar led the rally in currencies whose economies are closely tied to commodities. The Aussie rose to $1.0482 from $1.0456.

The Wall Street Journal Dollar Index, which measures the dollar against a basket of currencies, fell to 69.13 from 69.30 late Tuesday.

Electrolux Upbeat on U.S. Housing Recovery
By Simon Zekaria
812 words
17 September 2012
14:27
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LONDON—The American chief executive of Electrolux AB Monday said a recovering U.S. housing market will prove a boon to home appliance makers, even as the Swedish giant warned near-term demand for fridges, cookers and washing machines across the developed world continues to suffer.

In an interview, Keith McLoughlin also said the world's second-largest white goods maker after Benton Harbor, Mich.-based Whirlpool Corp. remains keen to buy bankrupt South Korean peer Daewoo Electronics as it pushes growth into the emerging markets, even though the company hasn't been made the preferred bidder in the sale process.

Earlier this month, the U.S. Federal Reserve moved to breathe new life into the housing market by buying mortgage-backed securities, but visibility on signs of life in the industry remain clouded amid tight credit and depressed new mortgage applications.

Still, Mr. McLoughlin is upbeat. "It is not a question of if the housing market is coming back, it's when—2013, 2014 or 2015. Once unemployment goes from 8.3% to 6% and the corresponding consumer confidence gets back up we think the housing market pops back. There is some pent up demand there."

However, for now the Stockholm-based company's first non-Swedish chief executive—Mr. McLoughlin is from Brooklyn, New York—warned demand across its debt-laden core markets of North America and Europe, where it generates the lion share of its sales, remains sluggish.

"I think until Western Europe, North America and other mature markets do their necessary rebalancing and deleveraging, and consumer confidence [rises] again [to lift] discretionary purchases, I don't think the market recovers."

Electrolux forecasts flat to 2% volume growth in the U.S. this fiscal year, with a boost coming from pricing gains. Mr. McLoughlin thinks U.S. volume growth is more likely to come in at the bottom end of the forecast. At best the company expects a 2% decline in volumes in Europe.

"The total market in Western Europe is declining for consumer, discretionary, durable goods—Southern Europe is spreading to Central Europe, spreading to Northern Europe and even spreading a bit to Eastern Europe."

"I think in the near term demand is getting worse as the contagion spreads, but given the actions being taken both in Europe and outside Europe we're optimistic that this will be cycle that will recover," he said.

The group targets growing middle-class affluence in Latin America and Asia to offset declining appetite for its vacuum cleaners and dish washers in mature markets. It aims to generate half its revenue in emerging markets by 2017, up from 35% today.

"We think we are very much on track. If we did no more acquisitions and just kept the historical growth rate of mature markets versus emerging markets, we would hit the 50%. We are [also] comprehending additional bolt on acquisitions."

One of those is Daewoo, even as national conglomerate Dongbu Group is reportedly in pole position with a bid over $300 million. "Daewoo has been for sale six different times. My advisers say this one isn't over until it goes to closing," said Mr. McLoughlin.

A positive outlook means the company is in a "growth phase", said Mr. McLoughlin, noting a considerable contribution from deal making activity. Electrolux last year made two buys to boost its emerging markets operations as it took hold of Egypt's Olympic Group and Chilean appliance company CTI.

"Of the 13% top line growth in the first half, six was acquired and six was organic. One was currency. We are keeping our eyes open."

Mr. McLoughlin said China, one of the biggest opportunities for the world's biggest appliance manufacturers, has seen a slowdown, but the size of the population will trump slack demand amid concerns over real estate over-construction.

"Has there been an over build and infrastructure over the last several years? It looks like to us there has been. Can that be relatively quickly absorbed with a market, economy and population demographic that large? We think it can. We think China like any other economy will go through cycles."

The company is also benefiting from more benign commodity prices, with steel prices falling as a result of weaker demand. But Mr. McLoughlin preaches caution given the company's wide materials exposure.

"The big trend in steel, which is our largest individual buy, is certainly positive. [But] it's going to be mitigated a little bit on certain plastics and resins. That is going up."

So while the company locks in 50% to 70% of its steel prices on annual hedging contracts, it takes a rolling three-month approach to plastics to respond quickly to market demand.

Katarina Gustafsson contributed to this article.

US
Housing on Mend, but Full Recovery Is Far Off
By Nick Timiraos
1028 words
14 September 2012
16:58
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Home prices during the first half of 2012 posted their strongest gains in six years, the clearest sign that more U.S. housing markets have hit bottom.

But the housing market remains far from normal. Hitting a bottom shouldn't be confused with a full-on recovery, which looks a ways off.

Today's rising prices have less to do with surging demand—though hard-hit markets in Arizona, California, and Florida have seen significant investor appetite for distressed homes—than with declines in the number of properties for sale.

Inventories of "existing" homes—that is, ones that haven't just been built—are at eight-year lows. New-home inventories are lower than at any time since the U.S. census began tracking them in 1963. In some cities, there are one-third fewer homes listed for sale than a year ago.

Here's why prices are rising: There are more buyers chasing fewer homes, and—critically—fewer distressed homes, such as foreclosures. Low inventory is one sign that housing markets may have reached a turning point because many want to buy at the bottom but few want to sell.

There are several factors behind the low inventory. Banks have slowed their pace of foreclosures. Investors have snapped up discounted properties that they can convert into rentals. Home builders, struggling for several years to compete on price with foreclosed properties, have added little in the way of new supply.

For now, price gains are concentrated at the low end of the market, where inventory declines have been most dramatic. "The market is really drying up in these seemingly distressed markets really quickly," said Michael Sklarz, president of research firm Collateral Analytics. "They really are scratching for properties to sell."

Low inventory is benefiting home builders, as buyers grow frustrated by bidding wars sparked by a shortage of move-in-ready housing. "People can't find inventory that they want, so they say, 'I'm just going to buy the house down the block that's brand new. I don't have to go through the whole torture,' " Mr. Sklarz said.

Housing's progress is good news for the economy. Residential investment has now contributed to U.S. economic output for the past five quarters, which hasn't happened since 2005. In other words, housing is no longer a drag, though it is packing far less of a punch than it normally does at this point in the economic cycle. Rising prices also could help turn around consumers' fragile psychology, an unpredictable but important factor that can fuel more sales.

But low inventory isn't necessarily a sign of strength. One problem is that many sellers can't or won't become buyers. Millions still owe more than their homes are worth, and even more—about 45% of all homeowners with a mortgage, according to data firm CoreLogic Inc.—have less than 20% in equity. That means they don't have enough money to make a large down payment and pay their real-estate agent's commission to buy a comparable house.

Large price declines have left cities without what historically has been the most active segment of the home-buying market: families looking to trade up and retirees seeking to downsize. That leaves many markets relying on investors and first-time buyers, who are most sensitive to rising prices and mortgage rates. Ironically, prices are rising fastest in markets that have the most underwater borrowers because so few homes are for sale.

While low inventories have helped firm up prices, they could also soon lead to year-over-year declines in sales volumes because there aren't enough homes on the market to sustain the current sales pace.

Consider Phoenix. Home prices through June were up by 17% over the past year, the best increase among the nation's big cities. But home sales in July fell 8% from a year ago, amid a drop in supply of more than 25%, according to a report from Mike Orr of Arizona State University.

Jon Mirmelli, a local real-estate investor, said, "Buyers aren't happy with what they see, and they're staying on the sidelines."

There are other reasons for caution. Banks are still stingy with credit. Many would-be buyers have too much debt to qualify for a mortgage.

A large overhang of distressed mortgages ultimately could drive more homeowners to sell or push banks to accelerate foreclosures. This "shadow inventory" looks as if it won't be dumped on the market in a way that would trigger deep price declines, but it would probably keep a lid on any swift gains.

Jobs and wages also aren't growing fast enough to sustain big rises in home prices. Recent gains may be less indicative of a strong recovery and instead point to how prices in some markets "overcorrected," bringing in investors who will step back as prices firm up.

Others worry that mortgage rates, which are down by a full percentage point from one year ago, are temporarily boosting sales and that housing demand will slump once rates rise. Compared with a year ago, mortgage rates allow borrowers to take out about 12% more in debt without increasing their monthly payment.

The changing debate over housing underscores the sector's tentative progress. Earlier this year, the question was whether housing would hit bottom this year or next. Now, it is "about how strong any recovery will be, how long it will last, and whether it will reach every neighborhood in America," said Glenn Kelman, chief executive of Redfin, a real-estate brokerage.

An important test comes later this year. In each of the past three years, prices rose in the summer but gave up all those gains and more in the winter, when sales traditionally slow. This year could be different because the supply of homes isn't piling up.

Absent a shock to the economy, housing is on the mend. But it will be a long time before it returns to normal.

Write to Nick Timiraos at nick.timiraos@wsj.com [mailto:nick.timiraos@wsj.com]

The Property Report: Europe's Property Gap --- Prices to Rise for Top Real Estate, Lower Quality Assets to Drop
By Anita Likus
851 words
12 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
24
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A mismatch between the kind of property that investors are seeking and what is available to buy is likely to push European real-estate prices lower for all but premium assets, according to real-estate analysts.

Also, the supply of property coming to market exceeds demand. European property valued at 413 billion euros ($526.9 billion) is going to come to the market over the next five years, averaging 83 billion euros a year, said Harm Meijer, European property analyst at J.P. Morgan Chase.

At the same time, just 126 billion euros of equity is available for purchases, with most buyers "targeting the type of assets that aren't up for sale," said Mr. Meijer. The investment of that equity is expected to be spread over a number of years.

The two biggest sellers of property will be banks, which continue to cut exposure to real estate, and governments on austerity drives, according to J.P. Morgan. Banks are expected to sell 231 billion euros in the next five years, while European governments should sell 117 billion euros of assets, J.P. Morgan says.

Overall, sales activity in Europe is far from the peak in 2006, when property valued at 246 billion euros was sold. In 2011, the total was 119 billion euros, according to figures from property adviser Jones Lang LaSalle.

Many of the properties being offered aren't the type most sought after by investors. For example, though European governments have some iconic properties in their portfolios, the buildings are either historic and need upgrades to bring them to modern standards, which makes them difficult targets for investors, or rundown and empty, which drives prices down.

This means the pricing gap between prime assets -- those of high quality in top locations -- and secondary assets will grow, Mr. Meijer said. "Prime assets will stabilize or rise in value, depending on the country, but secondary assets will continue to slide."

Some banks also are taking a hit on sales prices as they continue to remove property from their balance sheets. The U.K.'s Lloyds Banking Group PLC sold GBP 809 million ($1.29 billion) of Australian corporate real-estate loans for GBP 388 million in June to a joint venture of Morgan Stanley Real Estate Investing and private-equity giant Blackstone Group LLP. Lloyds is now in the process of selling a 2 billion euro Irish loan portfolio, dubbed Project Pittlane, which is expected to go at a hefty discount, a person with knowledge of the sale says. Lloyds declined to comment.

Other major sellers include publicly traded property companies, which will sell 8.6 billion euros of better-quality assets while they might become buyers for secondary property, said J.P. Morgan. Some 27 billion euros will come to market as the result of distressed mortgages that were converted into commercial mortgage-backed securities during the boom years.

U.S.-based private-equity giant Carlyle Group still has some 500 million euros to spend on offices, retail and residential buildings in top European cities for its third fund, which has already spent 1.7 billion euros on assets including hotels and offices. It is also working on a fourth fund, which will be similar in size to the third.

"We are convinced that there will be many opportunities for us coming from players' deleveraging needs," said Eric Sasson, managing director at Carlyle. "We are quite bullish on the largest economies in Europe: France, Germany, the U.K. and the Nordics," he added.

U.K.-based fund manager Schroders Investment Management, which has GBP 194.6 billion of assets under management, is also in the process of setting up a GBP 1 billion European fund, which it will invest in "low-risk and good-quality assets."

Most sales so far have been in Europe's largest and most-liquid markets, such as London and Paris. But the market is likely to see deals involving property located in southern European countries and older buildings in Germany.

J.P. Morgan expects 96 billion euros of property to come from Spain and 49 billion euros from Italy, two markets that have virtually collapsed, with few property transactions taking place currently. "We are looking for assets in Spain and Italy as long as we can build it into our pricing," said Markus Meijer, chief executive of U.K.-based private real-estate investment manager, Meyer Bergman.

Meyer Bergman this summer launched a 1 billion euro fund targeting retail property that is aiming to buy between 10 and 12 shopping malls in Europe. "We see a lot of appetite from U.S. investors wanting to buy in Europe, who previously focused on home markets," said Markus Meijer.

He said the closure of German open-ended funds will provide more buying opportunities. Such funds are likely to dispose of 31 billion euros of property within three to five years, said property-services company CBRE Group.

The Property Report
Sternlicht Eyes Sale of Stake --- Starwood Capital Hires Adviser to Search for Buyer of Possible Minority Interest
By Craig Karmin and Kris Hudson
729 words
12 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C11
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Starwood Capital Group, the private real-estate investment firm headed by industry veteran Barry Sternlicht, is exploring the sale of a stake in the company, according to people who have spoken with Starwood executives.

The Greenwich, Conn.-based firm has hired investment bankers at Morgan Stanley to conduct the search for a buyer, these people said. While it is unclear what percentage of the firm would be available, people familiar with Starwood's thinking said it is expected to be a minority interest.

Spokesmen for Starwood and Morgan Stanley declined to comment.

With about $19 billion in real-estate assets under management, Starwood is one of the world's largest property investors and focuses on higher-risk, higher-return property investments.

Mr. Sternlict, who built his reputation by investing in hotels and lodging, is credited with helping to popularize the boutique hotel concept with the creation of the W Hotel chain.

Real-estate executives say the sale of a general partnership stake can be a natural evolution for many private investment firms, including hedge funds and buyout shops. But the reasons for a sale can vary.

Some firms view a sale of a small interest to a longtime investor in their funds as a way to strengthen an existing relationship.

In most cases, these firms market stakes to large investors that already have made commitments to the asset manager and view an ownership stake as a way to share in the profits.

Carlyle Group LP, the Washington-based private-equity firm, sold a 5.5% stake to Calpers, a giant California pension fund, in 2001, and another 7.7% interest to Mubadala, an Abu Dhabi sovereign-wealth fund, before going public earlier this year.

Rival buyout firm TPG Holdings last year sold a 4.5% stake to sovereign-wealth funds in Kuwait and Singapore, which gave that private-equity firm fresh capital for new investments.

Some smaller investment firms have sold controlling interests in order to expand their footprint. In 2010, Simsbury, Conn.-based real-estate investor Landmark Partners sold a 55% interest to India's Religare Enterprises Ltd. to help the U.S. firm tap foreign markets and offer new products, Landmark officials said at the time.

Any Starwood sale would offer Mr. Sternlicht, the firm's chairman and chief executive, the opportunity to cash out some of his holdings in the firm he founded in 1991.

It also could establish a value for the company, which could prove useful if Starwood has any future plans of selling shares to the public.

A sale also would give a buyer a slice of Starwood's management fees and a share of Starwood's carried interest, or 20% of the profits that Starwood collects after certain performance hurdles are met.

Those management fees can range as high as 1.25% on real-estate assets that last year topped $18 billion, according to Starwood fund marketing material.

Starwood has raised about $2 billion toward its current fund, with a target amount of around $3 billion. The fund is focusing on distressed real estate and property loans.

Mr. Sternlicht spent much of the 1980s at JMB Realty in Chicago before striking out on his own.

He made his first mark during the commercial-real-estate collapse of the early 1990s purchasing properties on the cheap from the government's Resolution Trust Corp.

Later Mr. Sternlicht founded the Starwood Hotels & Resorts Worldwide chain and built it into a multibillion-dollar behemoth on par with giants Marriott International Inc. and Hilton Worldwide Inc.

Mr. Sternlicht left Starwood Hotels in 2005 amid a dispute with the board over his role and his choice of successor. Starwood Hotels now counts 1,112 hotels in 100 countries under its brands.

In recent years, one of Mr. Sternlicht's biggest deals was Starwood Capital's 2009 purchase of the $4.5 billion real-estate loan portfolio of Corus Bank, a failed condominium lender.

His firm also has purchased portfolios of midscale and upscale hotels, foreclosed on and revamped a Viceroy resort in the Caribbean and built his fledgling hotel brands Baccarat, Crillon and 1.

The Property Report
Firms Flock to Foreclosure Auctions
By Robbie Whelan
982 words
12 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

LITHONIA, Ga. -- On a muggy morning earlier this month, Paul Fuhrman pried the screen off a window to get into a two-story house in this Atlanta suburb.

It was just another day's work for the 43-year-old executive at private-equity firm Colony Capital LLC, based in Santa Monica, Calif. After buying the house for $120,000 in a foreclosure auction, Mr. Furhman and his colleagues wanted to check out Colony's new investment -- and broke in because they hadn't gotten the keys yet.

Mr. Fuhrman's sweat and dirty hands show how the business of buying foreclosed homes, renovating and renting them out is morphing from a largely mom-and-pop business into the next big thing on Wall Street. Investors who once chased only big-ticket deals now are buying houses one at a time.

"We went from hunting elephants to whacking ants with a mallet," says Mr. Fuhrman, who found stained carpets, piles of discarded clothing, a broken door and signs of a recent living-room camp out by squatters.

Colony owns about 3,600 foreclosed homes, including 133 bought in the Atlanta area in one day, and officials hope to increase the firm's inventory to 10,000 by next spring.

According to investment bank Jefferies & Co., major financial firms led by Colony, Blackstone Group LP, Och-Ziff Capital Management and Oaktree Capital Group LLC have raised more than $8 billion to buy houses, largely in markets pummeled by the housing crisis.

At first, many investors hoped lenders would sell foreclosed houses in bulk. But most banks prefer to sell one house at a time, figuring that approach will fetch higher prices.

As a result, the foreclosure circuit hasn't yet produced a giant windfall for buyers like Colony, though executives say early returns are promising. Yields on rents from houses owned by the firm are 7% to 8%, higher than many other types of real estate. Purchase prices have averaged 12% less than Colony expected, which should make it easier to sell the homes or borrow against them and exit with double-digit percentage gains.

Anthony Myers, a senior managing director with Blackstone's real-estate group, said that while the cost of operating in the foreclosed-home business is "incredibly high," the buyout firm is well positioned. "We, like others, believe in the housing recovery and we believe there's a great role for folks like us to come in, buy these homes," he said.

"We are in the sixth inning of the opportunity to buy distressed homes and rent them out," says John Burns, a real-estate consultant in Irvine, Calif.

Blackstone and Colony have been especially aggressive in foreclosure-racked Georgia. On a Tuesday morning, about 200 people gathered in front of the courthouse in Gwinnett County as an auctioneer opened the bidding on about 900 homes.

"Game on," said Albo Antonucci, a buyer working for Colony, rushing to join the crowd.

One early sale was a three-bedroom, two-bathroom house in Duluth, Ga. Bidding started at $43,515. Colony had been studying a published list of thousands of properties to be auctioned this month in the Atlanta area, driving by the houses and doing price comparisons to determine the maximum price the firm could pay for each house and still hit their target yields.

Jay Byce, a portfolio manager for Colony, glanced at his binder. It showed Colony's maximum bid price for the property was $71,000. After about 20 offers, he entered the bidding -- and won the house for $53,400.

In Georgia, buyers of foreclosed houses usually pay on the spot. Mr. Byce turned to a colleague who held a locked plastic case full of cashier's checks. In all, Colony had brought $3 million in checks to the courthouse. On the same day, 52 Colony employees were bidding on homes at seven different county courthouses in Georgia, and spent a total of about $9 million.

Under a red canopy tent, Evan Jaleh, a third-year associate at Colony, sat at a laptop set up at a card table, entering Colony's winning bids into a spreadsheet.

Nearby, Dallas Tanner, a principal with property-management company Treehouse Group, Tempe, Ariz., gave orders and distributed cashier's checks to his team, which usually buys using funds from Blackstone.

The surge in interest from financial firms has boosted foreclosure sales. About one-third of homes in recent foreclosure auctions in Gwinnett County were sold, up from 15% six months to a year ago, estimates auctioneer Doug Hardegree. Unsold homes go back to the lender.

The arrival of private-equity firms and hedge funds rankles smaller investors, many of whom try to quickly "flip" their purchases for a profit.

"If you've got four or five major investment firms bidding, you can't get anything out there," says Niana Hill, who sat in a courthouse cafe during a break in the auction. "I like 20% yields or more. I don't like 10%."

Colony executives say the numbers look fine to them. Once the private-equity firm spends about $11,000 to renovate the house in Lithonia, Colony expects to rent it for about $1,550 a month. After taxes, fees and other expenses, Colony investors should get a net yield on this house of 8.2% a year.

Colony has indicated it may take its rental business public or sell its homes after values appreciate. "When I look at other opportunities out there in the commercial-real-estate space, to get the same current yield, adjusting for risk, it's very difficult," Mr. Fuhrman says. "Of all the real-estate asset classes, this is probably the most liquid."

Seven years after buying the New York Marriott East Side and then plowing roughly $26 million into renovating it, Prime Property Fund wants to sell the Midtown hotel for up to $350 million.

Kris Hudson

Office Building Values are Flat…Again

Manhattan office building values in August remained stuck in the rut they've been in for about a year, according to a new report by Green Street Advisors.

The California firm's Manhattan Office Price Index—which tracks properties owned by real estate investment trusts—was unchanged last month at about 21% below the peak it hit in the middle of 2007. That's roughly the same level the index has been at since the summer of 2011, when Europe's financial problems bubbled over in a sign that the global economic downturn was far from over.

The good news is that commercial property values are well above the low point they hit in the middle of 2009. Since then they have risen 74%, according to Green Street.

"High-quality Manhattan office buildings remain a highly sought-after real estate investment amid exceptionally low interest rates," Green Street said in a report. "However, political and economic uncertainty are weighing on leasing decisions and are likely affecting investor confidence."

Values in Midtown have recovered more rapidly than downtown. Midtown office properties have bounced back 84% since the mid-2009 trough. Downtown buildings have recovered only 33%, Green Street reports.

Longer term, though, "Downtown's future looks brighter its past," the report states. "Over the next few years Downtown will benefit from infrastructure improvements," it says.

But the report also warns: "The World Trade Center towers remain a large source of space availability and uncertainty."

Peter Grant

Manhattan: 'Meh' on Malls

New York City's top shopping districts may boast the most expensive retail space in the world. But when it comes to shopping malls, the rents in the New York region rank far below those in other global cities.

That's one of the conclusions of a new report set to be released Monday by real-estate firm Cushman & Wakefield Inc. The average asking rent per square foot on Fifth Avenue between 49th and 60th streets fetched as much as $2,067 in the second quarter of this year.

But average rents at suburban-style enclosed shopping malls in this region were a mere $75 to $150 a square foot, the report states.

That compares with other U.S. cities such as Las Vegas and the Washington, D.C., metro area that are performing slightly better, at $200-$225 and $125-$135 per square foot, respectively. And in the second quarter of 2012, prime shopping center rents in Hong Kong were $927 per square foot per year, the report states.

The price variation partly reflects the preference many New York City dwellers have for ground-level stores. Vertical malls, especially in Manhattan, generally haven't been popular with shoppers.

But the city's hottest shopping areas see densely packed crowds and stores rivaling the most popular malls in the world. "What we're monitoring is density," says Matt Winn, the head of C&W retail for the Americas. "SoHo is a mall without a roof."

According to the report, the impact of globalization, e-commerce, and a shaky economy on retail has been polarizing: owners of prime shopping centers have had to tailor their tenancy to the greater number of consumers either at the high-end or discount segment of retail.

The report also finds that because traditional anchor stores like JC Penny and Best Buy are struggling with low revenue growth, mall owners are resizing physical spaces to reflect new visitor traffic drivers like Apple and Whole Foods.

"I think our lifestyles are changing," says Mr. Winn. "And that necessitates a whole new look and feel to the mall."

Kelsey Gee

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New New-Home Market --- Demand for Freshly Built Homes Is Heating Up, and Steep Bargains Are Vanishing Fast; Here's How to Get a Good Deal
By Ruth Simon
1845 words
8 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Six years after the U.S. housing market tumbled into the abyss, demand for brand-new homes is picking up. Sales are on the rise, and prices in many areas are inching higher.

The bad news for buyers: The new market environment means less discounting, fewer incentives and, in some cases, longer waits for homes. But there are several steps you can take to be a better shopper -- from choosing amenities judiciously to waiting until a home is partially built before pulling the trigger on a deal.

Low interest rates and improving economic conditions are persuading people like Jerold Hawkins to take the plunge. Mr. Hawkins, a software developer for the auto industry, and his wife, Katherine, a nurse, recently agreed to buy a new five-bedroom home in Rochester Hills, Mich., for $289,900. "I now have a really good job and so does my wife," Mr. Hawkins says.

The auto industry's recovery has spurred demand for new homes in the $180,000 to $500,000 price range in the Detroit area, says Dan Elsea, president of brokerage Real Estate One in Detroit. In the choicest neighborhoods, even homes built without a ready buyer often sell long before completion and with multiple offers, he says.

After the new-home market's dismal performance of the past few years, any improvement is welcome. The median price plunged 22% from its peak of $262,600 in March 2007 to a low of $204,200 in October 2010, with some markets tumbling 50% or more. Just 306,000 new homes were sold last year, says the U.S. Commerce Department, the lowest on record.

But the tide seems to be turning. New-home sales rose in July for the third time in four months. And with inventories at record lows, builders are trimming incentives and raising prices in markets such as Detroit, Houston, New York and Phoenix.

Overall, sales of new homes surged 22% from September through July, according to the National Association of Home Builders. The median price of new homes sold in July stood at $224,200, up 9.8% from their 2010 lows.

"We're clearly in recovery," says Douglas Yearley Jr., chief executive of luxury builder Toll Brothers in Horsham, Pa., which builds in 20 states nationwide. In August, Toll reported the most sustained demand in more than five years. Stronger markets include Connecticut, Massachusetts and New York, while Illinois and Las Vegas are softer, the company says.

Billie Armenta, a government employee, says she is surprised the $294,900 base price of her new home in Phoenix has risen by more than $33,000 since she and her husband, Ruben, a retiree, signed their contract in January. The couple had to wait six weeks for an appointment at the design center and nearly eight months for construction to be completed.

The much larger market for existing homes is perking up as well. Prices in June posted their first year-over-year increase in nearly two years, according to the S&P/Case-Shiller index of 20 major metropolitan areas. From September 2011 through July 2012, sales jumped 5%, according to the NAHB.

To be sure, the housing recovery is still in its infancy, with credit tight and sales and prices well below historical levels. Rising interest rates, an uptick in the supply of foreclosed homes or a weakening economy all could crimp sales.

But after years of retrenching, builders have whittled down their stockpiles greatly, and are being cautious about bringing new homes to market. The inventory of unsold new homes now stands at 144,000, down from a peak of 575,000 in July 2006, the Commerce Department reports.

At the current sales pace, that's a 4.9 month supply of new homes; six months is considered a balanced market, says NAHB chief economist David Crowe.

One of the biggest changes since the downturn is where new homes are being built. The new homes are located much more closely to job centers than they were five years ago, says John Burns of John Burns Real Estate Consulting in Irvine, Calif.

Some builders are rolling out features not likely to be found in the resale market. KB Home, which is based in Los Angeles and builds in 10 states and Washington, D.C., lets buyers choose whether the master bedroom is on the first or second floor. In Southern California, the company has consulted with feng shui masters on home design.

PulteGroup, which is based in Bloomfield Hills, Mich., and builds in 29 states, offers the "Pulte Planning Center," a secondary office space off the main living room that helps homeowners stay organized and monitor their children's homework.

The New Home Co. in Aliso Viejo, Calif., offers a multigenerational home with a private living space for an older parent or child returning home after college. "We can take any walk-in pantry we have and turn it into a separate cooking facility," says New Home Chief Executive H. Lawrence Webb. Lennar, meanwhile, offers a "Next Gen" home aimed at baby boomers with aging parents or boomerang kids.

Builders also are touting energy efficiency. Meritage Homes, which is based in Scottsdale, Ariz., and builds in seven states, lets buyers spend the credits they can apply to upgrades or financing on solar panels; in some model homes, an entire room is deconstructed to highlight energy-saving features.

Another change: Homes are starting to get larger again, after falling during the housing downturn. Among new homes sold in the first half of 2012, the median square footage was 2,013, up from a recent low of 1,945 in 2009, according to Hanley Wood Market Intelligence.

Experts say larger homes are more attractive to the trade-up buyers who now dominate the new-home market. Steve Ruffner, president of KB Home, Southern California, says buyers can afford bigger homes because prices and interest rates have fallen.

Of course, what you will get for your money depends on local conditions. If you are thinking about a new home, here are some points to consider.

-- Don't be fooled by the sticker price. "The base price always looks incredibly good," says Lloyd Fox, a real-estate broker at Long Realty in Scottsdale, but upgrades typically add 10% to 15%, sometimes more.

Billie Armenta, the Phoenix home buyer, says the purchase price for her home rose by more than $55,000 to $350,000 once the fee for a choice lot, carpeting, higher-quality cabinets and other upgrades were figured in.

-- Expect to pay a premium. The median new home sold for 36% more than the median existing home last year, excluding distressed properties that were foreclosed on, according to Hanley Wood.

That spread is wider than in the recent past. The gap had narrowed to just 28% in 2009, when builders were scrambling to unload inventory built during the housing boom. Since 2005 the gap has averaged 34%.

-- Remember that location matters. Your ability to negotiate a good deal can vary even within the same locale. "We have some markets where we're raising prices on every plan pretty significantly," Standard Pacific chief financial officer Jeff McCall told investors in July. "And then 10 miles down the road we have stagnant pricing."

Closer-in developments generally command a premium versus homes in outlying areas.

In Tucson, builders are offering discounts from the list price on homes built without a ready buyer in the Dove Mountain area, which was hard hit by the housing bust, says Laura Sayers, a vice president at Long Realty. Less than 10 miles away, in Oro Valley, demand for new homes is so strong there is a waiting list for new lots, she says.

-- Negotiate aggressively. Incentives are getting smaller, on average, but buyers in some markets still have a fair amount of bargaining power.

In the Tampa Bay, Fla., area, builders are buying down interest rates, paying closing costs and tossing in appliances, says Jaci Stone, an agent at Century 21 Beggins Realty, who had one builder kick in two months' rent so a client could break a lease.

Hayley and Tyler Sutterby recently nabbed $20,000 in upgrades instead of the standard $8,000 package when they bought a four-bedroom home under construction in Lithia, Fla. The couple is spending the credits on higher-quality cabinets and flooring, a tankless water heater and other features, says Ms. Sutterby, who works in marketing for a cable company.

-- Be tactical. The closer a home is to completion, the fewer choices you get to make, but the more likely the builder is to make a deal. That's because builders don't want to end up saddled with finished homes.

So keep an eye out for homes near completion or in inventory, says Ms. Stone, the Tampa agent, who adds that publicly traded builders are often more willing to make a deal at the end of a quarter or fiscal year.

-- Prepare to wait. When home prices were tumbling, buyers were regularly advised to sell their current homes before buying new ones -- or risk being stuck with two mortgages. But with inventories at record lows, buyers might have to wait three to six months for their new home to be completed.

That can make timing tricky for move-up buyers. Martin Mitchell, vice chief executive of Mitchell & Best Homebuilders in Rockville, Md., suggests that buyers in stronger markets prepare their home for sale, but wait until construction is under way before bringing it to market. Or sell your home and rent it back for a few months.

-- Understand your financing options. Builders often will pay closing costs or provide other incentives if you get your mortgage from them. But the deals offered by builders aren't always better than what you might get from another lender, says Greg McBride, a senior financial analyst at Bankrate.com.

Because their credit lines are limited, more builders are asking buyers to take out construction loans that roll into a standard mortgage once the home is completed instead of having the builder finance the construction, with the borrower taking out a mortgage when the sale closes. The bank makes payments to the builder, typically as construction goals are met, with the buyer generally paying interest only on the funds used.

Anyone considering this option, "should at least have some sense of what the permanent financing costs will be," even if you can't lock in a mortgage rate, to make sure payments are affordable, advises Keith Gumbinger, a vice president at mortgage-data provider HSH.com.

To reduce fees, arrange to close on both loans at the same time instead of closing first on the construction loan and then later on the mortgage.

Credit Markets
In China, Bond Rally Builds --- Developers Return to the Market and Find Strong Demand as Debt Sales Pick Up
By Fiona Law
710 words
7 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

HONG KONG -- Signs of a turnaround in China's property market have sparked a rally in bonds of the nation's developers, making them among Asia's best performers this year.

Midsize and large developers have benefited the most from investor demand, taking advantage of the interest by returning to bond markets after being largely shut out for several months.

The gains have come after some local governments in China have rolled out programs to encourage home buying while nationwide curbs on the property market remain in place. Lower interest rates and more mortgage lending have also contributed to the pickup in housing prices and transactions in the past two months after a decline in the first half. The rising activity has boosted builders' revenue and reduced their debt burdens. The rally in bond prices accelerated as the companies released earnings in the past few weeks.

"Investor appetite is there," said Anthony Arnaudy, Standard Chartered PLC's head of capital markets in northeast Asia. "The fundamentals of the Chinese property sector are healthier."

Bond prices of large developers such as Country Garden Holdings Co., Longfor Properties Co. and Shimao Property Holdings Ltd. have risen 40% to 50% since October. That has beaten the J.P. Morgan Asia Credit Index, which is up 17% over the same period.

Many investors say there is more room for developers' bond prices to rise, although fund managers are becoming cautious as the rally progresses, staying clear of smaller, debt-laden firms. Country Garden, Longfor and Shimao didn't respond to requests for comment.

Midsize developer Guangzhou R&F Properties, which focuses on middle-class residential projects, received strong demand last month for $238 million of bonds, receiving almost 10 times the amount on offer as investors were lured by the 11.875% interest rate.

Yu-Ming Wang, Manulife Asset Management's Asia head of fixed income, whose team manages $36 billion of assets, said he is buying bonds of property companies but is being choosy. "We remain highly selective and only pick the top half a dozen players with strong track records and good financial discipline," he said.

The pickup in the $36 billion Chinese property bond market is significant because it is watched by global investors eager for insights into the financial health of China's developers. The sector has been a big driver of China's economic growth, which expanded 7.6% in the second quarter compared with the year-earlier period. Most of these developers have below-investment-grade, or "junk," credit ratings. Demand for these bonds signals an increase in investor appetite for risky assets and a tacit, if nascent, endorsement of the property sector.

At the end of 2011, most bonds from Chinese developers were trading at distressed levels, as low as 60 cents on the dollar in some cases. Those low prices made them attractive to investors seeking high yields, especially as the worst of the global economic troubles passed, said some fund managers. Bond yields move inversely to prices.

However, "in a low-rate environment, capital is still looking for higher returns in the Asian high-yield market, and Chinese real-estate issuers have benefited from the bargain-hunting," said Angus Hui, Asian fixed-income fund manager at Schroder Investment Management (Hong Kong) Ltd.

Still, the market is far from healed after last year's rout.

While issuance has picked up, it still lags behind last year. Some 11 bonds valued at a combined $3.48 billion have been sold so far in 2012, compared with 18 bonds totaling $8.21 billion in the same period last year, according to data provider Dealogic.

Bankers don't expect a jump in issuance through the rest of the year. Many companies don't need the money, in part because they have scaled back expansion plans, and uncertainty about future government policy remains, said Augusto King, Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC's head of debt capital markets in Asia.

Esther Fung in Shanghai contributed to this article.
