The Property Report
Moody's Veteran Cracks the Whip -- Again --- Tad Philipp Grows Concerned About Retail Properties Facing Competition; Some Wall Street Debt Dealers Are Miffed
By Al Yoon
943 words
5 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Tad Philipp is at it again.

The veteran of Moody's Investors Service's commercial-mortgage group, who fought back against loose underwriting during the real-estate bubble, has drawn a new line in the sand against similar practices, leaving some Wall Street dealers miffed -- and itching to take their business elsewhere.

This time around, Mr. Philipp is growing more concerned about retail properties that are particularly vulnerable to competition from online shopping and nearby outlet stores. He is taking a more-cautious approach toward evaluating these properties, forcing Wall Street underwriters to boost credit protection for senior bondholders. That is reducing the firms' profits.

"We think we are at a fork in the road," Mr. Philipp said of retail property. Most luxury malls, as well as community centers anchored by grocers and other retailers of daily needs, will be fine, he said. But properties facing tough competition from nearby properties or with already low sales "are going to suffer," he warned.

Mr. Philipp's tougher standards hearken back to Moody's more-sweeping response in April 2007, as real estate was overheating with the help of commercial-mortgage securities, whose volume had more than quadrupled over the previous five years to $228 billion by 2007. Moody's began demanding more credit protection, turning underwriters to other rating firms.

By July of that year, Moody's market share had "done a complete flip," Mr. Philipp complained in a report at the time, accusing Wall Street banks of "ratings shopping."

"Moody's was one of the few firms who did something about aggressive underwriting, and the market was quick to punish them," said David Rodgers, a principal at Park Bridge Financial who managed Merrill Lynch & Co's commercial-mortgage securitizations from 2001 to 2009.

But Moody's, which rehired Mr. Philipp in March 2011 as director of commercial real-estate research after he did a three-year stint at CW Financial Services, is sitting with a luxury it didn't have in 2007. This time, Moody's has so far been able to act with impunity, with Standard & Poor's, its top competitor, hobbled by a costly move last year.

Dealers have shunned S&P on large, multiloan commercial mortgage securities deals for the past year, after the firm upended the market by yanking its ratings from a Goldman Sachs Group Inc. and Citigroup Inc. commercial-mortgage bond in July 2011. The failure to rate the Goldman-Citi issue came after a last-minute review revealed a disparity in its rating models.

An S&P spokesman declined to comment on Tuesday. Earlier this year, S&P launched an effort to overhaul its commercial mortgage bond ratings system.

Moody's was hired in S&P's place to rate the deal as Goldman and Citigroup revived its deal and the firm has been on every multiloan deal since.

"They [Moody's] know they will be on every securitization, along with Fitch [Ratings] or Kroll [Bond Ratings], so why not be super conservative?" asks Matthew Sharp, a managing principal with Hamilton Point Investments.

A handful of dealers say they are ready to bury the hatchet with S&P, however. Some said they would begin shifting business to S&P as soon as the firm finalizes its new ratings model, which is expected to be more lenient than Moody's. S&P has shown its new model would increase the portion of investment-grade ratings on some single-loan commercial mortgage securities it has rated.

Some Wall Street dealers said S&P might be hired along with Moody's on commercial-mortgage bond deals to test investor confidence in S&P. Indeed, J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. has asked S&P to rate an coming multiloan offering, according to people briefed on the deal.

Mr. Philipp and Michael Gerdes, who is the head of commercial-mortgage bond surveillance at Moody's, aren't fazed despite protests from Wall Street and the prospect of lost business. They say their skepticism of regional malls is warranted.

Messrs. Philipp and Gerdes also reject assertions by issuers that they weren't transparent with their views, which call for more "stress scenarios" on malls with disadvantages, such as living in the shadow of more-successful retail centers. The formula has been applied to troubled loans for years, so it shouldn't be a "foreign concept," Mr. Gerdes said.

Bond buyers wary of S&P's history of rating and criteria changes could demand more yield from S&P-rated bonds in the new and secondary markets, eliminating any advantage of avoiding the Moody's model, analysts say. Mr. Philipp said he has drawn support from buyers of riskier commercial-mortgage securities just as he has challenges from dealers.

"Moody's is now an investor favorite because history proved them right and the investors haven't forgotten that yet," said Mr. Rodgers of Park Bridge Financial

In a recent interview, Mr. Philipp reeled off examples of failing retail properties, which have resulted in some of the biggest losses in CMBS.

For example, part of the troubled Highland Mall in Austin, Texas, was sold in August for $1.5 million. That property was backed by a $61 million loan and the sale created one of the largest losses ever for commercial-mortgage bondholders.

International Property: Web Firms Find A Home in Berlin
By Vanessa Fuhrmans
638 words
5 September 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C22
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

For years after its restoration as reunified Germany's capital, Berlin built for an office-market boom that never took off. Its newfound status as Europe's Internet start-up mecca, though, may be changing that.

Lured by Berlin's modest rents and a creative class teeming with young, tech-savvy engineers and Web developers, Internet firms from Amazon.com Inc. to European online shoe retailer Zalando GmbH to Berlin-based website-clone incubator Rocket Internet GmbH have snapped up Berlin office space at furious pace over the past couple of years.

Some, such as Groupon Inc., have expanded so fast they have had to spread out over multiple offices in the city before finding new, bigger space.

The activity has propelled Berlin's office-rental turnover to record levels over the past year. In the first half of 2012, companies leased some 330,000 square meters (3.6 million square feet) of new office space, edging out Germany's reigning real-estate market, Munich, for the first time in more than a decade, according to property-services firm Cushman & Wakefield Inc. "Today, nearly "60% of our [new] customers are companies working in e-commerce," said Hanns-Joachim Fredrich, a Berlin partner at the firm.

That surge in demand, combined with investors' search for safer havens amid the debt crisis engulfing southern Europe, makes the capital ripe for a renaissance in office-building investment, some developers say. "After the disappointment of the 1990s boom, many foreign investors are taking another look at Berlin again," said Stefan Klingsohr, head of Berlin real-estate developer and consulting firm Klingsohr Gruppe.

A specialist in restoring old, historic buildings into shiny new loft-like commercial spaces, Mr. Klingsohr's company currently is renovating the second half of a prewar cigarette factory in Berlin's trendy Prenzlauerberg district and the century-old headquarters of what was once Germany's premier film studio, located near Checkpoint Charlie -- both of which will house primarily Internet companies.

A private British investor, which bought the former film-studio offices from a Commerzbank unit, is funding the restoration, which will provide about 11,000 square meters of commercial space, Mr. Klingsohr said. The developer is negotiating with several firms to lease offices there for between 15 euros ($19) and 18 euros a square meter -- above the city's average but below Berlin's top rental prices of about 21.50 euros a square meter.

Such small-scale historical renovation projects are typical of the nascent boom: Alongside another section of the former Wall, for instance, Berlin-based business "angel investor" JMES Investments and local property firm S+P Real Estate are developing an 8,500-square-meter start-up "factory" on the site of a former brewery. Berlin-based music-sharing platform SoundCloud and Firefox browser developer Mozilla Corp., which set up shop in the German capital this summer, will anchor what is aimed at operating as a hub for young technology firms.

But more-ambitious, new-building projects are rarer, evidence of Berlin's ever-fickle property market.

The city's still-low rents compared with more-affluent markets such as Munich and Frankfurt are part of the attraction for young, hungry start-ups. But they also reflect a local unemployment rate of 12% -- nearly double the national average -- and a big reason investors remain skittish about building big, new office towers.

"For 20 euros per square meter, no one could afford to build a state-of-the-art high-rise," said Mr. Fredrich of Cushman & Wakefield. "We don't have any industry sector here that is used to paying the rents it would take."

(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Average fixed mortgage rates in the U.S. retreated over the past week, halting a gradual rise seen over the previous month, according to Freddie Mac.

For the week ended Thursday, the 30-year fixed-rate mortgage averaged 3.59%, compared with 3.66% the previous week and 4.22% a year earlier. Rates on 15-year fixed-rate mortgages averaged 2.86%, versus 2.89% a week earlier and 3.39% a year ago.

Freddie Mac chief economist Frank Nothaft noted, "Treasury bond yields fell, allowing mortgage rates to follow" after the release of minutes from the Federal Reserve's monetary-policy committee. Mortgage rates turned lower despite continuing signs of improvement in the housing market in recent months, he said.

The release of Standard & Poor's/Case-Shiller home-price indexes for June earlier this week showed that U.S. home prices ended the second quarter with the first positive annual growth rates across the national, 10 major-metropolitan-area and 20-city indexes since the summer of 2010.

Five-year Treasury-indexed hybrid adjustable-rate mortgages, or ARMs, averaged 2.78%, compared with 2.8% the previous week and the 2.96% rate set a year earlier.

One-year Treasury-indexed ARM rates averaged 2.63%, compared with 2.66% the prior week and 2.89% a year earlier.

Asian Pacific Stocks: Chinese Stocks Fall, Japan Tech Plays Rise
By Daniel Inman
684 words
30 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
23
English
(c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

China's stock market fell to a fresh 3 1/2-year low, and mining stocks dropped, but Japanese technology stocks rose on deal speculation.

Moves in Asian benchmarks were muted as investors continued to wait for a policy signal from the U.S. Federal Reserve. Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke will speak Friday at an annual economic symposium in Jackson Hole, Wyo., and investors will look for any sign that the central bank could undertake additional measures to stimulate the U.S. economy.

"Low volumes and small moves in stocks are saying that no one has a clue on which way things should go," said James White, senior analyst at First State Investments, which has $150 billion of assets under management.

In Tokyo, the Nikkei Stock Average of 225 companies added 0.4% to 9069.81. Daikin Industries dropped 3.5% after the company said it had agreed to buy U.S. home air-conditioner company Goodman Global in a deal worth $3.7 billion.

The Shanghai Composite fell 1% to 2053.23, its lowest close since Feb. 2, 2009, on pessimism over the local economy.

Amid the downbeat sentiment, the influential state-run China Securities Journal urged China to cut banks' reserve-requirement ratio, adding that the effects of reverse repurchase offerings, the money market instrument used by China's central bank to pump cash into the market, are only short-term.

The report came after the People's Bank of China injected a net 278 billion yuan ($43.9 billion) into the money market last week, its biggest weekly liquidity injection in seven months.

In Hong Kong, the Hang Seng Index eased 0.1% to 19788.51 as the market digested more earnings reports from Chinese companies. China Life Insurance added 2%, as investors focused more on the 2.5% growth in new business value than the 26% slide in first-half net profit.

Evergrande Real Estate Group lost 3.1% after reporting that core earnings were down 21% in the first half, as the developer cut property prices to increase sales.

Want Want China led blue-chip decliners, tumbling 4.5%, as the snacks-and-beverages maker reported a 38% rise in its first-half net profit that fell short of expectations.

In South Korea, the Kospi added 0.6% to 1928.54, snapping a three-session losing streak. Samsung Electronics climbed 2.9% as it continued to rebound from Monday's 7.5% plunge on a U.S. court ruling that it had violated some patents belonging to Apple.

In Australia, a plunge in iron-ore prices to the lowest level since November 2009 dragged on producers of the commodity, especially the ones investing heavily to expand their capacity. Iron-ore pure plays Fortescue Metals Group skidded 6.4%, and Atlas Iron lost 3%.

Overall, the S&P ASX 200 inched down 0.1% to 4356.40.

In Mumbai, the Bombay Stock Exchange's benchmark Sensitive Index fell for the fourth consecutive session, losing 0.8% to 17490.81. It was the lowest close since Aug. 6.

Software company Infosys fell 1.4%, while state-owned Oil & Natural Gas Corp. declined 2.3%.

Asia Markets Finish Mixed
By Daniel Inman
442 words
29 August 2012
05:55
The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Asian markets were mixed Wednesday as the wait continued for a Federal Reserve signal, while there was a flurry of activity in Japan's technology sector. Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke will speak Friday at an annual economic symposium in Jackson Hole, Wyoming.

"Low volumes and small moves in stocks are saying that no one has a clue on which way things should go," said James White, senior analyst at First State Investments, which has $150 billion of assets under management. "It could change very quickly, as September could be a manic month."

The Nikkei added 0.4% to 9069.81, as a busy session for the technology sector spiced up the day's trading.

Renesas Electronics soared 35% after news that private-equity firm Kohlberg Kravis Roberts & Co. had approached the struggling semiconductor manufacturer with an investment offer. Shares in one of Renesas's major shareholders, NEC, climbed 6.5%.

Daikin Industries dropped 3.5% after the company said that it had agreed to buy U.S. home air conditioner company Goodman Global in a deal worth $3.7 billion.

In Hong Kong, the Hang Seng Index fell 0.1% to 19788.51 as the market digested more earnings reports from Chinese companies. China Life Insurance added 2%, as investors focused more on its 2.5% growth in new business value than its 26% decline in first-half net profits.

The Shanghai Composite was 1% down to 2053.23, due to pessimism over the local economy.

Australia's S&P ASX 200 inched down 0.1% to 4356.4, as a 4.6% overnight drop in the price of iron ore—to its lowest level since November 2009—dragged on producers of the commodity, especially the ones investing heavily to expand their capacity. U.S. Home Prices Ticking Upward
By Nick Timiraos And Kelsey Gee
841 words
28 August 2012
21:35
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

U.S. home prices in June posted their first year-over-year increase in nearly two years, according to a widely watched index released on Tuesday, as more buyers chased fewer homes on the market.

The S&P/Case-Shiller index of 20 major metropolitan areas showed home prices rose by 0.5% in June, compared with a year ago, to end a 20-month streak of declines. Home prices are still down by nearly 31% from their 2006 peak but have returned to mid-2003 levels.

A separate national index released by Case-Shiller said prices were up by 1.2% from a year ago and by 2.2% from the first quarter, on a seasonally adjusted basis. The quarterly gain was the largest since late 2005. "This is a clear trend now. We have had a very strong spring season," said Mark Fleming, chief economist at real-estate data firm CoreLogic Inc.

Tuesday's report follows several others that had pointed to an exceptionally strong bounce in sales this spring. The rebound began to take hold months ago, as the number of homes for sale dropped sharply, especially as banks have listed far fewer foreclosures. In addition, some traditional sellers are holding out for better prices or are unwilling to sell because they owe more than their homes are worth.

At the same time, investors have been aggressively scooping up bargain-priced foreclosures that can be converted into rental properties, while low mortgage rates are giving buyers even more purchasing power.

In each of the past three years, home prices have picked up in the spring and summer only to erase those gains in the fall and winter, when sales activity slows. But economists said there is a better chance the low inventories of for-sale homes and stronger demand could prevent prices from dipping below their previous lows.

"We have a much better supply-and-demand equilibrium right now," said Mr. Fleming. Compared with previous years' false dawn for housing, "we're much closer to the level of supply matching the albeit-muted level of demand," he said.

The report showed that price gains were increasingly broad based, though not every part of the country is benefiting. Of 20 tracked cities, 13 posted year-over-year increases, compared with just seven cities three months ago. Prices in Phoenix were up by 14% from a year ago, continuing a sharp turnaround for the hard-hit market. Prices were below last year's June level in six markets; the worst was Atlanta, which was down by 12%.

Since prices began falling in 2006, they had previously posted year-over-year increases briefly in 2010, when home buyer tax credits fueled a burst of sales activity.

The housing market still faces stiff headwinds, including tight lending standards, an economy that isn't producing large job or wage gains, and high levels of homeowners who are underwater, or owe more than their homes are worth.

Many economists say the recent price gains aren't likely to be sustained at the current pace. Instead, the sharp bounce may reflect the fact that prices in some parts of the country fell so far that they created unusually attractive deals. Some industry executives fret over why demand isn't higher given the low level of mortgage rates.

Another concern: While low inventory has helped push prices higher, it also has begun to crimp sales volumes, particularly in Western markets such as Phoenix, Las Vegas and Sacramento, Calif. Some economists say sales of previously owned homes could fall in the coming months, in part because there aren't enough homes for buyers to choose from. That could lift sales of newly built homes.

"To see real sales-volume gains, you're going to need to see more opportunistic sellers," said Glenn Kelman, chief executive of real-estate brokerage Redfin. "The only people selling are those who are bursting at the seams because they had a third baby or because they took a job out of state."

Jillian Holliday has no plans to move out of the house that she bought and renovated in Orlando last October, but that hasn't stopped her from trying to purchase nearby properties that could be fixed up and resold for a profit. She and her father offered to pay $230,000 for a tidy 1920s-era home in downtown Orlando the day it went on the market in April.

"There's almost nothing for sale in this neighborhood," said the 30-year-old recruiter. "And the houses you do see sell really quickly." The home is being sold as a short sale, where the seller must receive the bank's approval to sell at a loss. The last buyer walked away from the property after spending eight months under contract. Ms. Holliday hopes the sale will close by September. "It's such a gem," she said.

SYDNEY--The Australian dollar was weaker late Tuesday as global financial markets weighed the possibility the U.S. central bank will unveil further stimulus for the misfiring U.S. economy at the end of the week.

U.S. Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke is set speak Friday at an annual gathering of central bankers at Jackson Hole, where many expect him to announce more economic assistance.

Ray Attrill, the head of currency strategy at National Australia Bank, said the biggest risk for markets is in a possible failure by Bernanke to say anything new at the conference.

"If Mr. Bernanke fails to offer a strong signal that more Fed easing is imminent when he speaks on Friday, the U.S. dollar is likely to catch an across-the board bid," he said.

But for the moment, trading is subdued as northern hemisphere markets emerge slowly out of Summer holiday mode, he added.

Local economic news was scant Tuesday but one housing survey was negative for the Australian dollar.

The survey of sales of new homes by the Housing Industry Association showed a 5.6% decline in July from June, and an 11.6% slide on year.

The weak housing report challenges recent assertions by the Reserve Bank of Australia that 125 basis points in interest rate cuts since November were beginning to show up in stronger house prices and lending.

"Today's Housing Industry Association New Home Sales report for July is more sober suggesting demand in the new dwelling market remains more tenuous," said David de Garis, senior economist at National Australia Bank.

The longer-term view of the Australian dollar remains relatively robust.

Annette Beacher, head of economic analysis for Asia at TD Securities, expects the Australian dollar will trade strongly, averaging US$1.04 in the second half of 2012.

Strong central bank demand for the Australian dollar will sustain its strength in the future, she said.

"More than ever we are confident that central banks, global reserve managers and sovereign wealth funds are buyers of the Australian dollar on dips, hence it is extremely difficult to predict that the currency can sustainably trade back to US$0.90 and below in the next two years at least," Beacher said.

U.S. News: Sales of New Homes Climb Sharply

The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Sales of newly built homes rose briskly in July, and inventory fell to the lowest level on record, suggesting the housing market is showing continued signs of recovery and that builders may need to ramp up construction in the coming months.

The Census Bureau said Thursday builders sold a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 372,000 homes in July, up 26% from the same month last year. Inventory of new homes available for sale fell to 142,000 units, the lowest level recorded since the government started tracking the figure in 1963.

Economists were encouraged by the report. As home builders continue to deplete their inventories, they will "start construction on more new homes, and that's when construction will start to add to the economy," said Mark Vitner, senior economist with Wells Fargo Securities.

A separate report Thursday by the Federal Housing Finance Administration, which regulates mortgage companies Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, showed prices of previously owned homes rose 1.8% in the second quarter from the first-- the biggest quarterly jump in more than six years.

The rise in new-home sales partly reflects low stocks of existing, or previously owned, homes. On Wednesday, the National Association of Realtors reported that inventory levels for existing homes fell to a 6.4-month supply in July, from a 9.3-month supply a year earlier.

With so little available in the existing-home market, a growing number of buyers are choosing new homes, to the point where "new home sales growth is outpacing growth in traditional existing sales," says Ivy Zelman, an independent housing analyst who turned bullish on the home-building sector last year.

Potential buyers are "frustrated" with the existing-home market, says Scott Koppendrayer, director of sales for LDK Homes, a high-end custom builder based in St. Michael, Minn. "The stuff that's left, it's not the nice stuff."

Unlike existing homes, builders are holding the line on prices of new homes. According to the Census Bureau, the median price for a new home sold in July was $224,200, down from $229,900 in July a year ago.

Douglas Yearley, chief executive of Toll Brothers Inc., the nation's largest builder of large luxury homes, said his company is cautious about raising prices, because it still worries about driving away skittish consumers.

"We're still scared," he said in a conference call with analysts Wednesday. Still, Toll reported strong quarterly earnings Wednesday, with income rising 46% and orders for new homes surging 57% compared with a year earlier. Toll's numbers come on the heels of strong quarterly results for other big public builders, including D.R. Horton Inc., Meritage Homes Corp. and PulteGroup Inc.

Ian Pickering, a 40-year-old information technology director for a defense contractor, said he and his wife had looked at seven previously owned homes, all in the $200,000-to-$400,000 price range, in the last three weeks but were unhappy with the selection. "For the square footage we wanted, there was really nothing on the market," he said.

The Pickering family finally decided to look at a new home built by Ryland Homes, a large national builder. On Wednesday, the family closed on the purchase of four-bedroom, three-bath ranch house in Lake Nona, outside of Orlando, Fla., for $335,000.

REIT Upbeat on Rentals --- Associated Estates Realty Plans More Development, Purchases
By Ralph Winter
729 words
23 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Rental fundamentals are strong, leading management of Associated Estates Realty Corp. to feel confident about its strategy of developing and purchasing additional apartments, said Jeffrey I. Friedman, president and chief executive officer.

The REIT's apartments are practically all rented and tenants are paying higher rents, Mr. Friedman said.

Aided by good cost control, he said, AEC expects an about 20% rise in 2012 per share funds from operations, the industry's preferred gauge of profitability.

Mr. Friedman repeated an estimate that the REIT will produce funds from operations of $1.23 to $1.25 a share for all of 2012, up from $1.03 a share in 2011.

The 2012 estimate excludes three cents a share of charges for early repayment of debt.

The REIT sold 6,325,000 shares through a public offering in June and an additional 681,178 shares in the second quarter through an "At-the-Market" program, Mr. Friedman said, a common device in the REIT industry.

Total proceeds of $98.3 million were used to reduce debt.

Some analysts were critical of the expansion plans and sale of stock below per share net asset value.

Some suggested the REIT was overreaching in moving into several new markets, and were especially critical of a plan to develop 175 luxury apartments in Los Angeles.

A recent Raymond James report called management's plan "a scattered growth strategy" that holds down the AEC stock price.

Mr. Friedman strongly disagrees with that characterization, insisting that AEC "is following a well thought out strategic plan."

That plan involves diversification into promising new markets, he said, and selling older buildings while buying newer ones.

Associated Estates develops new properties only when low building costs permit the REIT to build more cheaply than buy, he said.

"One of the reasons we made it through 2008 and 2009 unscathed, without having to cut our dividend was because we stuck with our strategic plan," Mr. Friedman said.

The REIT has a 97% occupancy rate, he said, about as high an occupancy as can be achieved as renters leave and are replaced. "We're not offering concessions anywhere," he said.

New tenants pay 5.4% higher rent than the departing tenant paid, he said, and rents on renewals are 5.2% higher than for the expiring lease.

That trend appears to be continuing, he said.

Associated Estates owns 53 properties with a total of 14,114 units, he said, including a property in North Carolina where the transaction will close soon.

The REIT just completed developing a 242-unit project in Nashville and is building 99 apartments to expand a property in North Dallas.

Construction will begin this quarter on a high-end, 140-unit property in Bethesda, Md., Mr. Friedman said.

Next year AEC will begin construction of a 275-unit project in Dallas, he said, and 175 units in Los Angeles on "a great piece of property."

Those five projects will cost an estimated $235 million, he said, including $36 million already spent. This is a good time to build, he said. "The construction trades want work; it is still a buyer's market."

The REIT also this year bought or committed to buy $180 million of existing properties. Purchases in all of 2012 could total as much as $250 million, he said.

Possible tax changes after the November elections are motivating some owners to sell this year, he said.

The REIT this year sold four properties in western Michigan and one in Duluth, Ga., receiving $60 million for 1,192 apartments.

Average age of those properties was 26 years, he said.

Construction, purchases and sales all will contribute to a more diversified, newer and better balanced portfolio, Mr. Friedman said.

Midwest units produce 38% of the REIT's net operating income, he said, mid-Atlantic units 32%, Southeast properties 25% and Dallas 5%.

"Until 1998 we were 100% in the Midwest," he said, and as recently as 2005 about 75% of net operating income came from the Midwest.

Directors last March increased the dividend to an annual rate of 72 cents, he said, which is within the REIT's comfort range of 65% to 70% of generated cash.

Jobless Claims Up Slightly
By Jeffrey Sparshott And Eric Morath
744 words
23 August 2012
10:02
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

The number of U.S. workers filing applications for jobless benefits rose for the second straight week, a sign that the labor market, though improved since the spring, remains uneven.

Separately, sales of newly built homes in the U.S. rose in July, the third increase in the past four months, showing that the housing market is continuing its slow recovery.

Initial jobless claims, an indication of layoffs, increased by 4,000 to a seasonally adjusted 372,000 in the week ended Aug. 18, the Labor Department said Thursday. That's the highest level in about a month. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had forecast 365,000 new applications for jobless benefits last week.

Claims for the week ending Aug. 11 were revised up to 368,000 from an initially reported 366,000.

The four-week moving average of claims, which smooths out often choppy weekly data, climbed by 3,750 to 368,000.

A Labor Department official said there were no unusual factors in Thursday's report.

Jobless claims data have stabilized in recent weeks, and the broad trend appears to have improved since June, when claims ranged around 385,000 a week. Claims averaged about 366,000 a week in July and are around 368,000 a week so far this month.

Typically job creation increases when layoffs decline.

So far this year, hiring has been fitful. A separate Labor Department report out early this month showed that the economy added 163,000 jobs in July, the biggest monthly gain since February and a welcome relief after weak reports for May and June. But the politically important unemployment rate ticked up to 8.3%.

Policy makers at the Federal Reserve are closely monitoring the employment situation as they weigh another round of stimulus.

In minutes of their latest meeting, released Wednesday, officials signaled that they are readying new measures to boost the recovery unless data point to "a substantial and sustainable" pickup in growth.

The Fed's policy makers next meet formally on Sept. 12 and 13.

Thursday's Labor data showed the number of continuing unemployment benefit claims--those drawn by workers for more than a week--increased by 4,000 to 3,317,000 in the week ended Aug. 11. Continuing claims are reported with a one-week lag.

The number of workers requesting unemployment insurance was equivalent to 2.6% of employed workers paying into the system in the week ended Aug. 11, unchanged from the prior week.

New-Home Sales Gain

New single-family home sales increased by 3.6% last month from June to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 372,000, the Commerce Department said Thursday. The rate matched May's level, which was the best since April 2010.

Sales were up 25.3% compared to the same month a year ago.

The results beat expectations. Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires had forecast an annual sales rate of 360,000.

June's sales rate was revised up to 359,000 from a previously reported 350,000.

July's gain continues momentum for new home sales after a downturn in June.

Sales of previously occupied properties also bounced back last month. Existing home sales increased 2.3% in July, a fairly modest improvement from June, which was the worst month of 2012, according to a National Association of Realtors report released Wednesday.

Still, prospective homeowners face tightened credit restrictions that make it hard to secure record-low mortgage rates.

New-home sales peaked in July 2005, when they hit an annual pace of nearly 1.4 million, and declined sharply in the run up to the financial crisis. Last year's level of 306,000 sales was the lowest on record.

The Commerce report showed the median price for a new home in July was $224,200, a 2.5% decrease from the same month a year ago. July's median price was the lowest since January.

The number of new homes listed for sale, seasonally adjusted, at the end of July was 142,000. That's the lowest level on record.

The supply would take 4.6 months to deplete at the current sales pace.

July's new-home sales were mixed in four U.S. regions. Sales grew 76.5% in the Northeast and 7.7% in the Midwest, but fell 1.6% in the South and 0.9% in the West.

The Property Report
Rich Folks Go Where Pensions Dare Not
By Craig Karmin
815 words
22 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

As big institutional investors pull back from investing in high-risk real-estate funds, these funds are turning to a new source for capital: rich people.

Starwood Capital Group, Lone Star Funds, Carlyle Group and others have raised billions of dollars over the past several months from wealthy individuals seeking to get in on the firms' newest "opportunity" funds, which buy or develop riskier properties and use higher levels of debt in hopes of reaping high returns.

Traditional investors in opportunity funds -- public and corporate pensions, foundations and endowments -- reduced their allocations to the funds or began avoiding them altogether after the funds racked up huge losses during the real-estate downturn that started in 2008.

"In their place, you've seen high-net-worth investors," said Barry Sternlicht, chairman and chief executive of Starwood Capital. While pensions and other large investors can require a lengthy review period before their boards approve any new investment, wealthy individuals are appealing in part because they can act quickly. "They are very fast, flexible and intuitive," Mr. Sternlicht said.

Mr. Sternlicht declined to discuss details of his firm's fundraising. But people who have spoken with Starwood officials about the latest opportunity fund said it raised about $800 million from high net-worth individuals. That accounted for about 40% of the total $2 billion raised, up significantly from its previous fund. Starwood's fund is targeting distressed properties and debt, and new construction, including a condo and hotel project near the Brooklyn Bridge.

Carlyle raised about 10% of its $2.3 billion fund from high-net-worth individuals, while Lone Star took in more than $1 billion of a $5.5 billion fund that closed last year from these investors.

Even Blackstone Group, which has had no trouble attracting institutional money for its current real-estate fund, has turned to individuals to diversify the investor base. More than $1 billion of the $13 billion that Blackstone is expected to raise has come from high-net-worth individuals, said people who have spoken with Blackstone officials.

Real-estate executives say the case for commercial-property investment is easy to make when compared to other investments.

Bonds, for example, offer skimpy yields that range from about 1.8% for 10-year Treasury debt to around 3% for a corporate bond. The stock market has been volatile.

Meanwhile, property prices are well below their highs, and many funds are targeting returns of about 15% or higher.

Most individual investors didn't participate in the commercial-property bubble, so they haven't been as shell-shocked as pension funds have after it burst, noted Vincent Costantini, chief executive of Boston-based property investment and advisory firm Roseview. "They had been staying on the sidelines," he said.

Affluent individuals are contributing from around the world, with increasing participation from wealthy Asian families and businessmen, private-bank officials said. In the U.S., they range from young entrepreneurs with new windfalls to retirees.

The growing prevalence of individuals investing in real estate has also been a boon for the private-bank divisions of J.P. Morgan Chase & Co., UBS AG, Citigroup and other big banks. These banks funnel money from their private-bank clients to the funds, earning a generous referral fee of as much as 4%, which is paid by the client and the fund.

Wealthy investors typically need to have a net worth of at least $5 million to invest in opportunity funds and must invest at least $250,000.

So far, the banks are referring clients primarily to brand-name real-estate funds with multiyear track records of good performance. That means hundreds of first-time property funds looking for investors are essentially cut out of the action.

Ultra-high-net-worth investors with Citi Private Bank, who typically have between $25 million and several billion dollars in assets, are interested in this product, said David Bailin, global head of managed investments. Citi partners with funds that can "source excellent transactions" and focus on primary markets like London and New York, he said. The sponsor needs to be reputable and also of "institutional quality."

Drawing from wealthy individuals carries some risk. Individual investors are more likely than institutional investors to run into financial trouble that could cause them to renege on their commitments to inject promised capital. That is something that rarely happens with a pension fund.

Some real-estate advisers say funds that rely too heavily on individual investors can have a harder time raising institutional money. Pensions and other big investors require more disclosure and regular reporting information than individual investors, and they like to invest alongside others that share their interests.

---

Julie Steinberg contributed to this article.

Heard on the Street / Financial Analysis and Commentary
1341 words
21 August 2012
The Wall Street Journal Asia

English
(c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

Winds May Be Shifting

For China's Real Estate

Rising house prices mean headwinds to China's growth are blowing less fiercely.

On average, residential property prices in 70 major cities in July were up 0.14% from June. It was the second monthly increase after eight months of falling prices. Sales are also inching higher. The amount of floor space sold in July was up 14% from a year earlier, the first increase since the end of 2011.

So far, a turnaround in real-estate sales isn't translating into new investment. New starts were down 30% in July from a year earlier, a sharper fall than in previous months. Land sales were down 9%.

With real-estate investment the mainstay of China's domestic demand, that helps explain why growth is tepid, and everyone from cement manufacturers to electrical-appliance retailers is having a hard time. Falling land sales also explains why local governments, which rely on such sales for around 35% of their revenue, have been slow to push the stimulus button.

Behind the scenes, though, there are signs that investment is starting to pick up, too. China Overseas Land & Investment, one of the country's largest developers, has adjusted its sales target for the year to 100 billion Hong Kong dollars ($12.89 billion), up from HK$80 billion. Du Jinsong, China real-estate analyst at Credit Suisse, says many developers are planning to accelerate new starts to meet stronger demand.

There are also signs of recovery in land sales. The premium that developers pay over the starting price at land auctions has been on the rise, from a low of 1% in February to 11% in July.

Don't expect a surge in land transactions. Christie Ju, real-estate analyst at Jefferies, says years of unused land bank means developers will remain cautious. But rising competition means there is hope that land sales in the second half of the year will at least be stronger than in the first half.

A livelier land market will mean local governments have more to spend. Stronger construction should start to feed through into orders for China's industrial sector, too.

Both sectors are rebounding from a low base. But China's government will hope that a headwind to growth in the first half will be turning to a tailwind for stabilization in the second.

-- Tom Orlik

Risks for Capital Spending

To look at the woeful earnings season that's wrapping up, the days of capital expenditure bolstering the U.S. economy seem numbered. But don't count it out yet.

S&P 500 earnings likely increased by just 0.9% in the second quarter versus a year earlier, according to S&P Capital IQ's latest estimates. For the third quarter, analysts are forecasting the first outright decline in profits since 2009.

Spending on new equipment and software is usually a function of demand: Whatever direction earnings growth is going is where capital spending will head within the next quarter or so. Profits began to seriously weaken in mid-2007, for example, and by early 2008 capital spending had begun to shrink. So even though spending on equipment and software grew at a healthy 7.2% inflation-adjusted annual rate in the second quarter, according to the Commerce Department, its future pace is in doubt.

But Europe's downturn and Asia's slowdown explain much of the weak growth in U.S. company earnings. So the dynamic isn't the same as it was in the last recession.

In the second-quarter survey of chief financial officers he conducted with CFO Magazine, Duke Fuqua School of Business economist John Graham found stark differences in the capital-spending plans of U.S. firms booking most of their sales overseas and those with more domestic exposure. CFOs at companies booking more than half their sales in Europe expected spending to fall by about 5% over the next year. Companies with less than half their sales in Europe expected spending to increase by about 10%.

U.S. exports are substantial, but the bulk of foreign sales by U.S. companies come from the operations they have in place overseas. For example, the Pepsi sold in India is made in India. U.S. Trust chief market strategist Joseph Quinlan estimates that such sales came to more than $6 trillion last year. That compares with U.S. exports of just over $2 trillion.

That matters because just as overall profits dictate how much money companies spend, they tend to spend it in places where they are doing well. The fact that General Motors' European operations are struggling, for instance, didn't stop it from renovating and restarting production at its former Saturn assembly plant in Spring Hill, Tenn.

If there is a risk to capital spending in the U.S., it is less what profits have been and more what would happen if Congress can't prevent the $1.2 trillion in automatic spending cuts scheduled to start in January. Given how reliant many equipment and software firms are on defense and other government spending, that's a potent threat.

-- Justin Lahart

ECB: Europe's Conditional Bank

Another day, another gain in Spain. But are investors in the country's rallying government bonds getting carried away about European Central Bank bond purchases?

The assumption is that the ECB will buy bonds, and the euro-zone crisis debate seems increasingly centered on the finer details of how such bond buying might actually work. Spanish Finance Minister Luis de Guindos has called for the ECB to commit to big purchases even before Spain asks for aid. Der Spiegel magazine in Germany has resurrected the debate about the ECB capping yields for struggling euro-zone states. But there is no such thing as a free lunch. Both of these ideas fail to take account of a key ECB requirement: conditionality.

That is surprising. After all, the one thing the ECB has been explicit about is that any intervention will come with strings attached. President Mario Draghi has made clear that before the ECB will buy a country's bonds, its government must first apply to the euro-zone bailout facilities for aid. Mr. Draghi has said that even then, purchases of short-term debt might not be automatic: An application for aid is a necessary but not sufficient condition.

Conditionality is a particularly vexing issue for the ECB. In October 2010, after the central bank's first foray into bond buying, politicians moved from ruling out a euro-zone debt default to arguing that private-sector creditors could be required to take losses if countries asked for aid. That led to the debacle in which the ECB refused to take losses on the Greek bonds it had purchased, and other investors suffered even greater losses as a result. Then, in 2011, governments in Italy and Spain quickly backpedaled on overhauls after ECB purchases started. ECB executive board member Joerg Asmussen warned Monday that this mistake must not be repeated.

So Mr. de Guindos's call for an open-ended commitment looks like a nonstarter. If that were made, it would be almost impossible to force Spain to reform as the threat of the ECB discontinuing its purchases would be hollow. That leaves the ECB with a challenge. The idea of conditionality looks difficult to square with yield caps or any other form of fixed target.

In fact, conditionality isn't a happy partner for monetary policy, the banner under which the ECB has said bond purchases are possible. It pushes the unelected ECB even deeper into the political arena. And it raises the question of what the ECB would do if, once purchases have started, a country fails politically or economically to achieve its targets. For if conditionality is to mean anything at all, there must be consequences for failure.

-- Richard Barley

Today's Markets
Markets
Stocks Inch Lower
By Matt Jarzemsky
606 words
20 August 2012
17:13
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

NEW YORK—U.S. stocks took a pause, with the Dow industrials ticking down for the first time in three sessions, as telecommunications and consumer-discretionary shares fell.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average gave up 3.56 points, or less than 0.1%, to 13271.64. The blue-chip benchmark had capped its sixth-straight weekly advance on Friday, putting it within striking distance of multiyear highs.

The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index shed 0.03 point, or less than 0.1%, to 1418.13. Best Buy tumbled 10%, its biggest decline since December, as buyout talks with founder Richard Schulze deteriorated and the company named Hubert Joly, the head of hospitality and restaurant giant Carlson, its chief executive. The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.38 point, or less than 0.1%, to 3076.21.

"The markets may be a little bit tired--obviously we closed at near four-year highs for the S&P and the Dow Friday," said Ryan Larson, head of equity trading at RBC Global Asset Management. "Markets run, and they take a few breathers."

European markets slipped, with the Stoxx Europe 600 down 0.5%, after the European Central Bank played down reports it was preparing to intervene in sovereign-debt markets. A spokesman for the central bank said a weekend report about such plans in German news magazine Der Spiegel was "absolutely misleading."

Spain's IBEX-35 stock index, which had gained on the Der Spiegel report, reversed and was down 1.2%.

"There have to be some real long-term structural changes in how Europe attacks debt and growth," said Ernie Cecilia, chief investment officer at Bryn Mawr Trust Co., which manages more than $6 billion in assets. "Everything we've observed so far hasn't supported that there's the political will to do that."

No major domestic economic data were scheduled for release.

In corporate news, shares of Coventry Health Care surged 20% after the company agreed to be acquired by Aetna in a deal valued at $5.7 billion in cash and stock. Aetna rose 5.6%.

Lowe's dropped 5.8% after reporting disappointing quarterly results and issued a downbeat outlook for the full year.

Facebook reversed losses to rise 5%. Earlier, it touched an all-time intraday low of $18.75, less than half the social network's initial public offering price of $38. Traders said the stock's drop might have encouraged buyers. Meanwhile, Capstone Investments raised its stock-investment rating to "buy" from "hold."

Facebook had stock plunged 10% over the previous two sessions after lockup agreements expired, making some 271 million additional shares eligible for sale.

Apple added 2.6%, giving it a market capitalization of more than $623.5 billion, the highest ever for a U.S. company. The former record holder, Microsoft, had a value of $616.3 billion in late 1999.

Elsewhere, Asian markets were mixed. China's Shanghai Composite slipped 0.4% after average housing prices rose in July, fueling worries that policy makers there will continue with measures to cool the property market. Japan's Nikkei Stock Average edged up 0.1% to a 3 1/2-month high.

Crude-oil futures lost less than 0.1% to settle at $95.97 a barrel, while gold futures added 0.2% to finish at $1,620.10 an ounce. The dollar lost ground against the euro and the yen. The benchmark 10-year Treasury note rose 2/32 in price to yield 1.812%.

World News
Dutch Far-Left Leader Warns on Cuts
By Maarten van Tartwijk
620 words
19 August 2012
13:10
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

ARNHEM, The Netherlands—The current front-runner in next month's Dutch general elections warned Sunday that a single-minded focus on austerity is crippling the country's economy and could unravel the euro zone.

"I can't say if we will be able to maintain the euro. The European economy is hurting too much from austerity," Emile Roemer told reporters at the launch of his Socialist Party's election campaign.

Mr. Roemer's far-left party has benefited from growing voter resentment toward the German-led austerity drive and euro-zone bailouts. Some polls give it a clear lead over the Liberal Party of Prime Minister Mark Rutte.

Mr. Roemer blamed the current problems partly on flaws in the euro zone's design and said "technocrats" have gained power at the expense of voters. He said struggling countries in Southern Europe should be allowed more leeway.

"Europe should be about people, not about multinationals and the financial sector," he said.

Mr. Rutte's minority government collapsed in April after talks on new austerity measures fell apart. An interim coalition government agreed on a package to reduce the budget deficit to below a European Union-prescribed limit of 3% of gross domestic product in 2013.

Polls show Dutch voters—frustrated with austerity and wary of bailing out Southern European governments—may reject the package.

The Socialist Party, which didn't support the austerity plan, would win the most seats in the 150-member Parliament, about 36, or four more than Mr. Rutte's Liberals, a poll released Sunday by website Peil.nl showed. A week earlier, the Socialists had a lead of six seats, according to Peil.nl.

The Netherlands is still widely considered one of the stronger economies in the euro zone, with low unemployment and a decadelong track record of fiscal prudence.

But the EU expects the country to be one of the region's weakest performers this year, as spending cuts and a property slump squeeze domestic demand.

The Socialist Party doesn't reject the EU budget rules, but wants two more years' time to implement them. "We will make different choices," said Mr. Roemer said. "It's not the pace of austerity that matters, but the quality."

In a bid to revive the economy, Mr. Roemer wants to increase spending on infrastructure and schools. He also seeks to reverse a planned increase in the retirement age, raise the minimum wage, and undo spending cuts in social security and health care.

Mr. Roemer, a former elementary-school teacher, has become popular because of such clear-cut language and his often cheerful, informal demeanor. But critics say his election promises could spell trouble for public finances. Finance Minister Jan Kees de Jager, a vocal advocate of budgetary discipline, said last week that Mr. Roemer's comments are causing unease in financial markets.

The Netherlands is currently in the midst of a property downturn that shows some similarities to—if not the severity of—the one that has pushed Spain into a deep economic crisis.

A housing and property boom in the 1990s and 2000s that was fueled by cheap credit has come to an abrupt end, resulting in falling house prices and a sharp contraction in the construction sector.

The slump is hurting consumer sentiment, as many households have started saving more to pay off their mortgages.

Some of the country's major banks last week reported rising bad loans, both on mortgages and commercial property. The local banking regulator has warned that a deepening downturn in the property markets could aggravate the problem.

Markets
Treasurys Extend Retreat
By Cynthia Lin
580 words
16 August 2012
16:46
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

NEW YORK—Treasurys slumped for the fourth-straight session as investors lifted yields to their highest levels in more than three months to better reflect the slow-but-progressing economic recovery.

With euro-zone developments quiet, the focus has been on U.S. conditions and the possibility of more monetary stimulus from the Federal Reserve. Not all economic releases have been positive, but the trend is one of gradual improvement.

Investors are increasingly convinced that the housing market has found a bottom: A report Thursday morning showed housing starts slipping in July, but new permits rising to the highest level since August 2008.

A separate report showed weekly jobless claims rising, though the smoother indication from its four-week moving average fell to its lowest level since the end of March.

Encouraging data, along with the Fed's own wait-and-see approach, have somewhat deflated expectations that the central bank will deliver another bond-buying stimulus program at its September policy meeting.

"The Fed hasn't come out to make a bold statement about what they're going to do, and that has really dampened expectations they'll do anything at all," said Joe Burke, interest-rate risk manager at Interactive Brokers Timber Hill Group.

But Mr. Burke isn't convinced of the recent optimism surrounding U.S. data, pointing to discouraging manufacturing reports. He believes the 10-year yield will reverse lower and test the 1.4% lows reached just three weeks ago.

The Philadelphia Fed published its business index Thursday, showing another month of contraction in the mid-Atlantic region's manufacturing activity. This followed a sharp drop reported Wednesday in a similar survey conducted for the New York region.

The safe-haven market enjoyed only brief support from the discouraging report.

By late-afternoon trading, those gains had evaporated. Benchmark 10-year notes lost 10/32 in price to yield 1.842% after touching a three-month high of 1.861%. The 30-year bond shed 29/32 to yield 2.960%. Bond yields rise when prices fall.

Bond analysts expect 10-year yields to hug the 1.80% level, a point they say looks to be the new fork in the road as investors gauge U.S. economic conditions and tweak expectations for additional easing.

The Fed's policy-setting committee meets next on Sept. 12-13, when many market watchers had anticipated the central bank to deliver another cheap-money initiative to support growth.

Two Fed members, known for their vigilance on inflation, told The Wall Street Journal that they question the good more bond-buying would do for the economy. Philadelphia Fed President Charles Plosser called the potential move "dubious," while Dallas Fed President Richard Fisher said recent economic data show no urgent need for action.

With the Fed's next move still up in the air, plenty of attention will be placed on the central bank's annual Jackson Hole, Wyo., symposium at the end of the month. It was here two years ago that Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke gave off a clear signal that the second round of quantitative easing was on its way.

This wait-and-see mode, of course, only holds barring fresh headlines out of Europe. News about the region's ongoing debt crisis has been fairly quiet this month, allowing investors to focus on economic progress in the U.S.
